On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to the price stated (which is
published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
PITMAN’S SHORTHAND BOOKS
Every enterprising man catches short-
hand, like measles, at a certain stage of
growth ; and Pitman’s is, on the whole, the
best system to catch. It is simple, scientific,
and reliable.
The books recommended to the student on
commencing the study of Phonetic Short-
hand are the “ Phonographic Teacher,”
“Key,” and “Progressive Studies,” or
“Pitman’s Shorthand Instructor” and
“ Key.” The art may be learned from these
books without the aid of a personal teacher.

REVIEW.
WEBSTER’S
INTERNATIONAL
Dictionary.*
BY BRUCE SMITH.
The above is the title of the latest edition of the
labors of Noah Webster, whose name as a lexi-
cographer has become familiar wherever the
English language is spoken. Speaking generally,
it is indeed a most wonderful literary production,
ind offers a splendid illustration of that process of
growth which has had applied to it, in late years,
the term “evolution.” Originating in a modest
spelling book, published when Webster was a mere
student, the work has developed by different stages,
during the century or more which has since passed,
into a tome of over 2000 close pages of three
columns each; replete with every addition and
advantage which scholarship, literary skill, and a
keen appreciation of the requirements of modern
life can conceive. The spelling book was published
in 1783, when Webster was 25 years of age ; and
soon afterwards it was suggested to him that he
should compile a dictionary; but, being of opinion
that he was “ not qualified by research ” to enter
upon such a project, he did not follow the sugges-
tion, though the idea seems to have taken root in
his mind. In 1793, 10 years after the spelling
book had been got out of hand, he entered upon
the more ambitious undertaking which Dr. Johnson
has said to be the appropriate work of a “harmless
drudge ” —that of writing a dictionary. He
“ spent 10 years in a comparison of radical words,”
and in forming a synopsis of the principal words in
20 languages, arranged in classes under their
primary elements. In 1806, as the result of 10
years of silent but arduous work, Webster pub-
lished his “Compendious Dictionary,” the fore-
runner of the work which was completed in 1828,
and which he estimated to contain “ 70,000 or
80,000 words in all their various applications—
popular, scientific, and technical”; and he modestly
concluded his preface to that edition by expressing
the hope that, if the talent entrusted to his care
had not been put to the most profitable use, he
hoped it had not been kept laid up in a napkin.
Meanwhile, Webster was depending for the entire
support of his family on the proceeds of the spell-
ing book, of which it is estimated that 24 millions
of copies were sold down to the year 1847, the
demand at present being about one million copies
a year. The second edition of the dictionary was
published in 1841, Webster having meanwhile
visited Europe for the purpose of perfecting his
labours. He died in 1843, having completed a
supplement to his work, which was published just
before his death.
• • •
Revisions of the dictionary were published in
1847 and 1864, with regard to each of which
neither enterprise nor money seems to have been
spared to bring the work up to the most modern
ideas of completeness. The present edition pro-
fesses to be, and indeed is, a substantial advance
on its predecessors; so that the ponderous tome
which lies before us may be truly said to be the
evolved result of a century’s labor and research of
many minds, originating in a student’s modest
attempt at a school spelling book.
• * •
The competition among publishers of the pre-
lent day in the production of what are termed
“ books of reference ” is indeed keen ; and their
enterprise may almost be said to have belied the old
ma.vim that “ there is no royal road to learning.
A dictionary, in the more restricted sense, is
merely a book of words. Dr. Johnson, indeed,
said, in speaking of his own work, as a compiler
of one : “ I applied myself to the perusal of our
writers, and, noting whatever might be of use to
ascertain or illustrate any word or phrase, accumu-
lated, in time, the materials of a dictionary.” In
that sense, indeed, such a work would merely give
the origin, meaning, pronunciation, and spelling of
words of ordinary use, leaving what may 1"
called technical terms for works of a sped 1
character; and in many oases, such as those in
which the meaning involved a detailed explana-
tion, the encyclopaedia, which is of necessity a
bulky work, would have to bo resorted to. W e
fire, however, living in what is called an 44 ad-
vanced age,” in which everyone possesses a smat-
tering of scientific knowledge; and we are, too,
living in a rapid age—the age of steam and
electricity, and of the telephone—in which know-
ledge, when required, must be obtainable “im-
mediately.” Brevity, besides being the soul of
wit, is the watchword in every branch of know-
ledge in this present day—the portal of the 20th
century. Out of all these conditions and circum-
stances a certain class of wants is springing up.
These wants seem to have been understood by the
publishers of Webster’s Dictionary, and their
publication seems to be an enterprising attempt to
meet all requirements. The modern dictionary
must contain everything which the most exacting
can demand; and the matter must be complete
and advanced, as well as being as full as possible,
without entrenching unduly on the province of the
encyclopaedia. The comparison of the work under
review with the celebrated dictionary of Dr. John-
son well illustrates the change from the spirit of
social and industrial repose of the 18th century to
the hue and cry of 19th century competition, with
all its inevitable unrest. If, nowadays, in the
course of conversation some scientific term is used
in business hours, or some author or public char-
acter referred to in public life, or some literary
reference made over a dinner table, there must be
a ready and realistic method of obtaining the
knowledge which “the pace we live at” has
prevented us from previously acquiring. In
Johnson’s time, it was unobtainable, except by a
roundabout process, sometimes only by enquiry
from specialists. Nowadays it must be obtained
in a few seconds ; and for ready comprehension, if
the matter be of a technical nature, probably, an
illustration must be supplied.
• • •
We know of no book of reference that more com.
pletely complies with these modern requirements
It is in every sense of the phrase “up to date.”
Its impartiality, too, which is a matter of impor-
tance, is well testified to in the fact that a Homan
Catholic Archbishop in these colonies has said of
it : “ In case of words having a special reference
to the Roman Catholic religion, the narrow and
stupid bigotry displayed in other dictionaries finds
no place.” Turning to the volume itself, round
about which we have so long philosophised, we
are at once struck with its cleverness of mechan-
ical form. Life is too short to admit of all the
2000 pages being turned over in the ordinary way
to find the letter under which the needed reference
lies ! Niches are made for every letter of the
alphabet, so that, the thumb-nail being inserted,
the volume may be thrown open in the “twinkling
of an eye ” at the exact spot required; and each of
these niches is firmly bound in leather to prevent
wear. Apart, however, from this cynical ground
of utility, there is another not readily seen.
When one is Dent on some inquiry, it is confusing
to have simultaneously to occupy the mind in a
lengthy turning of leaves in the search ; and this
mental inconvenience is avoided by this method o
rapid reference. The mechanical method referred
to is sufficiently clever to have been patented in
that country where human ingenuity seems t Q
obtain the largest amount of protection from in-
dustrial pirating.
# • *
Turning to the body of the dictionary, in order
to give some general idea of its contents, we are at
once struck with the immense amount of informa-
tion which it affords, outside the primary purpose
of such a work, and yet of a character which
cannot for a moment be considered inappropriate.
The publishers have wisely reprinted, as a fitting
commencement of the work, the different prefaces
which have appeared with the several previous
editions. These have a literary and historic value,
serving to mark more clearly the different stages
of growth through which the work of lexicography
has passed. Hollowing upon this is a comprehen-
sive collection of all the arms and flags of various
nations, including not merely the greater nations,
but such comparatively unimportant ones as
Samoa. Some idea may be obtained of the
extent of the labor involved in the work by the
list of authors—about 2500 in number—whose
writings have been used as authority for or in
illustration of the forms and uses of words in-
cluded in the dictionary. These writers in some
cases date back into the thirteenth century, and
others come down to our own generation. Hollow-
ing on these is a brief history of the English
language, by a well-known professor, of Yale
College. In this history the influences of the
Celtic, the Latin, the Scandinavian, and the
Norman-Hrench languages are successively traced,
as also are the rapid changes in the English
language during the five centuries subsequent to
the Conquest. The difficulties, as well as the
importance of pronunciation are sufficiently recog-
nised in the fact that no less than 30 pages of the
work are devoted to a treatise on the subject.
Some consolation will be afforded to bad spellers
when they learn that no less than 1500 words in
the language, of which a list is given, may be
spelled in two oi three ways. Regarding the body
of the dictionary itself, too much cannot well be
said.
• # *
We note that so high an ar \rity as the Times
said of it that it was the “best and most useful
dictionary of the English language ever pub-
lished,” and the Quarterly Review, no mere
handier of undeserved compliments, has spoken of
it as “the best practical diction ry extant.” It is
difficult to say more than this, but it is not diffi
cult to justify such praise.
# * •
And here again we are drawn into a comparison
for the purpose of estimating the advance that
lexicography has made since Johnson s time.
When that writer compiled his famous dictionary,
he offered excuses for his imperfections, on the
ground that he “could neither visit caverns to
learn the miners’ language, nor take a voyage to
perfect his skill in the dialect of navigation, nor
visit the warehouses of merchants and shops of
artificers to gain the names of wares and tools and
operations of which no mention is found in books.
A reference to the work under review will satisfy
anyone that all these difficulties, which perplexed
Johnson, have been completely removed by
Webster and his succeeding editors. The preface
of this work says : “We find ourselves compelled
to yield to the necessity, which in these days
requires that the dictionary, which is ever at hand,
should carefully define the terms that record the
discoveries of science, the triumphs of navigation,
and the revelations of life.” Specialists have, it
is claimed, been enlisted in almost every branch
of human knowledge, e.g., astronomy, archi-
tecture, biology, botany, chemistry, law,
mathematics, medicine, engineering, mechanics,
mineralogy, music, nautical terms, geology, art,
zoology; and, as a consequence, the “miners
cavern ” has been visited, and all its terms have
been defined ; a voyage has been taken, and thg
“ dialect of navigation ” has been investigated
and explained in every detail. This constitutes
an immense stride from the one work to the other.
As an instance, the dictionary contains illustrations
of every conceivable kind of vessel or boat; and
under the head of “ ship,” not only every part of
the hull is defined and its use explained, but every
sail and every rope is carefully named, in such a
way that the necessity for “ taking a voyage ” is
entirely removed for the purpose of understanding
the meaning of technical terms connected with
seafaring matters. In the same way commercial
and industrial terms are made perfectly clear,
without the “visits to the warehouses,” which
Johnson deplored his inability to undertake. Wliile
Johnson confesses his inability to ascertain the
names of tools, &c., the editors of the dictionary
have evidently taken pains to supply them, as, for
instance, under the heading of “ file,” no less than
nine different illustrations are given to explain
the various kinds and uses of that tool.
m * *
To attain to such perfection much money must
have been spent and much trouble involved ; and
we can readily believe the publisher s statement
that “upon technical subjects eminent specialists
have been employed.” There appear, indeed, to
have been 100 paid editors engaged on the last
edition. So much as to the comprehensiveness of
the work. Where words have gradually acquired
a variety of meanings, as used in different senses
and by different writers, great care has been
taken to facilitate reference. As illustrations of
this care in detail, the verb “ fall,” picked at ran-
dom, is defined in nine different ways; and in each
case a quotation is given from some recognised
writer in which the word is so used. The adjective
“ clear,” also chosen at random, has 11 meanings
attached to it; and, again, in each of the 11 mean-
ings, a quotation is given from some well-known
writer. It would be an interesting inquiry to
ascertain how far the principle of recognising a
word as part of our language, when once it has
been used by a well-known writer, ought to be
carried. Carlyle and Meredith (the novelist) are
both included in the list from which quotations
are taken; but it would scarcely be considered
allowable to include in the dictionary some of the
erratic verbal creations of either the novelist or
the philosopher referred to. Carlyle frequently
indulges in the use of the words “ beautifuller ”
and “beautifullest” as the comparative and super-
lative of “ beautiful;” and Meredith is equally
fertile in verbal vagaries ; but, notwithstanding,
Webster’s Dictionary does not include either of
these words, and we certainly think the editor
has shown judgment in the omission. Although
great writers help to make the language,
some good judgment is necessary in accepting
all their verbal creations. Again, in cases
where a word of a technical character is used
in three or four scientific senses, the same careful
treatment in giving the different meanings at-
tached to the word is discoverable, as, for instance,
the word “ expansion,” which has attached to it
its common meaning, its meaning in mechanics,
its meaning in reference to mechanical engineer-
ing as well as in connection with naval architec-
ure; and with this word, as with others, an
appropriate quotation is given by a recognised
authority.
* # *
In relation to modem scientific terms the work
lacks nothing. As an illustration, we may men-
tion the word “ differentiate,” which in the Im-
perial Dictionary is to be found only in the
Appendix. In Webster it stands in the body of
the work, and is defined in three separate ways, as
used in logic, in biology, and in metaphysics. The
practice of introducing illustrations into the dic-
tionary is.apparently much extended ; and a novel
feature consists of indicating the natural size of the
object illustrated by figures which signify the pro-
portion the illustration bears to the object repre-
sented, as forinstance, “l-6th”or “l-10th,”indicat-
ing that the illustration is one-sixth or one-tenth of
the natural size. If space would permit, many in-
teresting facts might be recorded as to the value
of the main part of this great work. To this,
however, there is added an Appendix of upwards
of 300 pages, containing a variety of most useful
information, all, as we have said, being within the
limits of appropriateness, and being presented in a
form at once brief and sufficient for the purpose of
ready reference. First is a list of noted fictitious
persons and places, including pseudonyms and
surnames, as well as a large number of nicknames
which have been applied to eminent persons. The
principle of this division is to first give the name
itself of the fictitious character, then the name of
the book in which the character appears, and
finally a brief account of the most marked charao
teristie associated with the character. Many
characters in modern fiction are absent, as, for
instance, “John Halifax”; but, on the other
hand, the collection is most complete in characters
from what may be termed the modem classics
in fiction, such as Thackeray’s “Pendennis,
Dickens’s “Pecksniff,” and Bulwer Lytton’s
“ Claude Melnotte.” Remembering Napoleon’s
saying that “ nicknames should never be despised,
as it is by such means that mankind are governed,”
the nicknames applied to eminent men or well-
known groups of men should form an interesting
study. We might instance the instructive his-
torical term, “ Adullamites, ” which was originally
applied by John Bright to a small political party,
the circumstances of which application are given
opposite the name.
• * •
Following on this list comes a Gazetteer, contain-
ing 25,000 names of places of interest. By the use
of a few simple signs, which, for convenience, are
repeated on every page, the area and population
are given, as well as the pronunciation of the
name, and the main natural and artificial features
of each place which it designates. Beyond this is
a biographical branch, containing about 10,000
names of noteworthy persons, dead and living,
showing their nationality, station, occupation and
the date of their birth and death. Hundreds of
living persons are included, and the comprehen-
siveness of the list may be judged by the fact that
it includes the names of such “ lesser lights ” as
William Black, novelist, and M. Blouet (Max
O’Rell). After these comes a complete list of
Scripture proper names, with their proper pro-
nunciation, and a collection of Greek and Latin
names, in regard to which the authority of the
most eminent scholars of the day seems to have
been consulted. A pronouncing dictionary of
common English proper names, with their deriva-
tion, signification, diminutives and nicknames, as
also equivalents in other languages, and an ex-
haustive collection of quotations in Greek, Latin
and modern languages, such as frequently occur in
literature and conversation, are also included.
• * •
Even at the expense of lengthening this notice,
we cannot omit to refer to the fine series of pic-
torial illustrations, which fill nearly 100 pages,
and comprehend about 5000 different subjects,
with (in some eases) detailed information concern-
ing each. It would be impossible to give a fair
idea of this portion of the work, which contains a
most complete series of illustrations with reference
to the human body in relation to its anatomy, its
physiology, and its nervous system. Its plates on
architecture constitute a perfect object-lesson
education in the classic and modern styles. It
contains 1200 illustrations in botany, 300 in
geology, heraldry, mechanics, machinery, hydrau-
lics, and 300 in zoology. Perhaps the highest
practical tribute which could be paid to the value
of Webster’s work is the fact that (we quote from
the publishers’ notice) since the expiration of the
copyright of the above-mentioned earliest edition,
fac-simile reprints of the old editions have been
issued to the world, with all their since-discovered
errors and defects, under various titles, which are
calculated to deceive the many. We look forward
with pleasure to a further perusal of and frequent
reference to the work; and we can heartily wish
it all success, as the monumental result of a worthy
and laborious career.
♦ “Webster’s International Dictionary of the English
Language,” being the authentic edition of “Webster’s
Unabridged Dictionary,” comprising the issues of 1854,
1879, and 1884 ; now thoroughly revised and enlarged
under the supervision of Noah Porter, D.D., LL.D., of
Yale University, with a voluminous Appendix. Published
by G. and 0. Merriam Co., Springfield, Mass.; 40 Hunter-
street, Sydney ; 439 Little Collins-street, Melbourne,—Re-
printed from the Sydney Daily Telegraph, October 27,
1894.

that it “ solemnises marriages free of charge in
urgent oases.” As only “urgent” marriages
eventuate in these hard times, how does the con-
cern get its living ?

terminal stations on FRIDAY, 14th DECEMBER, 1894, and thereafter by all trams until

JANUARY, 1895. _

on the Sydney Suburban and Newcastle Suburban Sections on 25th, 26th IXECEMBER, 1894, an(/
Ist JANUARY, 1895, will be available for return until day following date of issue.

My Horse .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I OWN a tall, domesticated horse of the semi-angu-
lar type of beauty. He has an open countenance,
a hollow back, and an expressive hazel eye. ' A
former owner of the horse removed the other eye.
This person was vexed and mortified because he
could not catch the horse, and in the heat of the
debate assaulted the animal with a 'gun. The
horse has been sceptical about the trustworthiness
of human nature ever since. I have owned him
for a long time now, but I have not yet won his
entire confidence. His name is Parkes.
He associates with a number of illiterate quad-
rupeds that roam the bush here, and they all
regard Parkes as their chieftain. When any
member of the mob finds a patch of couch-grass,
Parkes depresses his ears and sidles alongside with
a sour look. If the animal knows Parkes he goes
away. But if he stays there, Parkes bites his neck
and kicks him until he does adjourn. Then my
horse eats the grass in a thoughtful way, and after-
wards gets another horse to reverse ends with him,
and wmsk the flies off with his tail. But Parkes
does not whisk for the other horse. He is too
languid.
Many people think it is easy to catch Parkes,
but they are wrong. They are deceived by the
statuesque attitude he assumes when another horse
is being caught. But when you approach Parkes
with a bridle, he smiles satirically and goes away
quite rapidly. That is, if you have only a bridle.
But if you have a tin dish of com, he takes the
com with him. He gets it by degrees from your
hand, and when you grab for his front hair he
ducks and cross-counters with his front paws. He
is very quick with his left.
Last winter I made a mg for Parkes with two
flour bags and a clothes-line. He was pleased
with the effect and at once struck up an intimacy
with a neighbour’s cow, although before that he
was a very bashful horse in female society.
The neighbour’s cow’s name is Mike. Her
owner was facetious and named her Mike Howe.
But (he is dead now) I am told he was otherwise a
most estimable man, and a model husband and
father.
Mike and my horse became very affectionate,
and my horse got quite vain and haughty, because
of the distinguished look the mg gave him. But
one day, when he was trying to help Mike’s calf
into a lucerne paddock, the rug became dis-
arranged. The ropes straddled his back, and the
main body of the mg clung confidingly to his
abdomen. When he next called on Mike, she
received my horse coldly. He was hurt at this,
and came home to me to have his garb refitted ;
but I took it off and made it into a meat-safe.
Mike never afterwards associated with my horse.
She even pretended that she did not recognise
him, and dropped his acquaintance altogether.
Her cruelty broke my horse’s heart, and ne has
never since recovered his old gaiety of spirit. He
pushed Mike’s calf down a shaft last week; but
he is still gloomy and abstracted.
I think his disposition is permanently soured.
A. CHEE.

When Parkes was at Broken Hill, four
years ago, he “ laid ” the foundation-stone of a
trades hall. Next day, before the old man had
gone, somebody capsized the stone and stole the
buried coins. Just now men earning 3s. a day
maximum at cracking stones, dumped cm the
trades hall site, use the stone as a “spell-of ” seat.
The treatment of Piles by the new Cannalin
remedies is necessitating the dispatch of large
quantities to all parts of the colonies. Applica-
tions must be made direct to the depot at 257
George-street, Sydney, N.S.W., where a copy
of Mr. Sharp’s popular work on “ Piles ” can be
obtained free.
“ Convict Jim and Other Poems,” by Will
Crompton (Maryborough, Q.), is a collection of
fluent doggerel—chiefly imitations of Gordon,
Bret Harte, “Dagonet,” and others. Occasion-
ally, as in “The Toast of a Darkened Soul,” the
imitation goes as far as absolute plagiarism. The
writer complains that friends tempted him to pub-
lish against his better judgment. He should have
rested his defence on the fact that he is an aider-
man ; an alderman is capable of anything.

The London School Board elections, held
amid intense excitement, have resulted in a big
victory for the Progressive party (secular-educa-
tionists) as opposed to the “Moderates” (religious-
educationists). On the old board the Moderates
held 34 seats, and the Progressives 19. The
numbers are now: Moderates, 29; Progressives,
26. Free thinkers all the world over (not the
sect, but the men) have cause for congratulation.
It is notable that the smaller parties—R.O.’s,
labour, and Socialist—couldn’t get one candidate
in.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city TifK Bulletin is not delivered by mail, l)ut is entirely
in the ha nds of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted, —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. AU
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Jilach and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical dra wings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
an I interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be p lid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn — will* be.
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal :—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also ;— R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

and South Australia,

postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments

outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 2s. 2d. added for
postage.

prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

In the Days When the World
Was Wide.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The world is narrow and ways are short, and our
lives are dull and slow,
For little is new where the crowds resort, and less
where the wanderers go;
Greater, or smaller, the same old things we see by
the dull road side —
And tired of all is the spirit that sings of the days
when the world was wide.
When the North was hale in the march of Time,
and the South and the West were new,
And the “ gorgeous East ” was a pantomime, as it
seemed in our boyhood’s view ;
When Spain was first on the waves of change, and
proud in the ranks of pride,
And all was wonderful, new, and strange, in the
days when the world was wide.
Chen a man could fight if his heart was bold, and
win if his faith was true—
Be it fame, or honor, or pow’r, or gold, or all that
our hearts pursue—
Could live to the world for the family name—or
die for the family pride—
Cquld fly from sorrow, and wrong, and shame, in
the days when the world was wide.
They sailed away in the ships that sailed ere
science controlled the main,
When the strong, brave heart of a man prevailed
as ’twill never prevail again ;
They know not whither, nor much they cared —
let Fate or the winds decide —
The worst of the Great Unknown they dared, in
the days when the world was wide.
They raised new stars on the “ silent sea ” that
could fill their hearts with awe,
They came to many a “ strange countree ” and
“ marvellous ” sights they saw.
The villagers gaped at the tales they told, and
old eyes glistenedbvith pride—
When barbarous cities were paved with gold, in
the days when the world was wide.
’Twas honest metal and honest wood—in the days
of the Outward Bound—
When men were “ gallant ” and ships were “good ”
—roaming the wide world round.
The gods could envy a leader then when “ Follow
me, lads ! ” he cried —
They faced each other and fought like men in the
days when the world was wide.
They tried to live as a free man should ; they were
happier men than we.
In the glorious days of wine and blood, when
liberty crossed the sea,
’Twas a “ comrade true ” or a “ foeman ” then, and
a trusty sword well tried.
(They faced each other and fought like men in the
days when the world was wide.)
The “good ship” bound for the Southern seas
when the beacon was Ballarat!
With a “ Ship ahoy !” on the fresh’ning breeze,
“ Where bound ?.” or, “ What ship’s that ? ”
The “ immigrant train ” to New Mexico, the
“ rush ” to the Lachlan side—
Ah ! faint is the echo of “ Westward Ho ! ” from
the days when the world was wide !
South, east and west in advance of Time —and,
ay ! in advance of Thought!
Those brave men rose to a height sublime —and is
it for this they fought ?
And is it for this damned life we praise the god-
like spirit that died
At Eureka Stockade in the “roarin’ days” with
the days when the world was wide ?
We fight like women and “feel” as such, the
thoughts of our hearts we guard,
Where scarcely the scorn of a god can touch the
sneer of a sneak hits hard.
The treacherous tongue and cowardly pen—the
weapons of curs decide—
They faced each other and fought like men in the
days when th® world was wide.
Think of it all —of the life that is ! Study your
friends and foes !
Study the past! and answer this: “ Are these
times better than those ?”
The life-long quarrel, the paltry spite, the sting of
your poisoned pride !
No matter who fell, it were better to fight as they
did when the world was wide.
Boast as you like of yotir “mateship” now-
crippled and mean and sly—
The lines of suspicion on friendship’s brow were
traced since the days gone by.
There was roqm in thqlong, free lines of the van to
fight for it side by side—
There was beating room for the heart of a man, in
the days when tire world was wide.
With its dull, brown days of a shilling-an-hour
the dreary year drags round—
Is this the result of “Old England’s” power?—
the bourne of the “ Outward Bound ?”
Is this the sequel of “ Westward Ho !” of the days
of “ Whate’er betide !”
The heart of the rebel makes answer, “ No!
We’ll fight till the world grows wide !”
The world shall yet be a wider world—for the
tokens are manifest ;
East and North shall the wrongs be hurled that
followed us South and West.
The march of Freedom is North by the Dawn !
Follow—" whate’er betide !”
Sons of the Exiles, march ! March on !—march
till the world grows wide !
HENRY LAWSON.
Two Cockies.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A long view of dusty road; two heavily-laden
waggons, each drawn by three horses ; two men
trudging steadily beside them with shouldered
whips—as fine a pair of lithe, six-foot Australians
as could bq found between Wilson’s Promontory
and Cape York.
They were on their way to the Riverina to
select on some land that had just been thrown
open. Their father’s selection of 320 acres was
too small to hold these two and four brothers —a
fact recognised some years previously, and making
them set to at once, working as hard as the devil
for souls, to earn enough money to buy drays and
horses and keep for some months.
At the end of a week they arrived, being first on
the field —which surprised them a bit. They had
the pick of the place, and selected so as to have
the middle of the hut exactly on the boundary
line of their holdings, not considering it worth
while to build two huts at first. In six weeks
they had six acres cleared, chock-and-log fenced,
and sown with wheat.
The iifixt Sunday they were sitting on a plank
outside the hut, smoking, and discussing with
satisfaction their future prospects.
‘ ‘ Hullo, I wonder who they are ?” said Bill,
spying two horsemen in the distance.
‘‘They seem to be coming here,” said Tom, as
ho leant forward to look. “So they are; we’ll
soon see what they want, anyhow.”
Then they shut up and smoked.
When the horsemen came closer, one of them
was seen to be a gentleman—that is, judging him
in the modern fashion, by his clothes ; the other
dressed, and looked, the overseer. Bath were
mounted on splendid horses, whioh Tom and Bill
admired before they even looked at the riders;
and Tom continued his admiration through the
first question.
“ What are you fellows doing here?” asked the
gentleman, pulling up in front of them. By his
tone they guessed at once that he was the owner
of the station on which they had selected.
Bill narrowed his eyes, looking up rapidly from
the horses’ legs, and drawled vlry slowly:
“ Having a spell.”
The squatter’s face reddened a little.
“ Gorge, now ! I don’t mean that —what are you
doing on this land ? ”
“ Clearing it,” drawled Bill, more slowly still.
The squatter looked very mad, very red, and
began to speak rapidly.
‘ Do you know you are on my property and
have no right here ? ”
“ No, I don’t.”
“ Well, I tell you so ; and you’ll have to get off
it: do you understand ?”
“ Why didn’t you tell us that a month ago?
Your overseer knew we were here. But this isn’t
your property either; it’s ours now ; so don’t try
any bluff with us or we’ll warn you off; and if
you don’t go, prosecute you for trespass.”
The squatter urged his horse forward a step or
two and raised his riding-whip. Bill never moved ;
but the squatter saw a look come into his eyes that
made him lower the whip hastily, and pull back
again.
Tom rose slowly, walked into the hut, and came
out with a double-barrelled gun.
“You fellers had better get,” he said—and they
did.
* * *
In another six weeks Bill and Tom had
cleared and sown another six acres, still keeping
to the plan of half on each selection. Their first
crop had sprouted a few inches, when, one morn-
ing, they found it eaten to the roots and some
sheep still nibbling at it.
Bill walked over and looked at the gap in the
fence and then called out: “I say, Tom, some of
those mangy station-hands have done this ! ”
Tom was quietly driving the sheep out; as soon
as he heard this he sooled his dog on to them,
which worried three to death.
Bill and Tom set to work at once repairing
damages, when they saw the overseer coming across
■ the paddock.
“ I wonder how much that fellow had to do with
this business ? ” said Tom.
“ Very little, I think. I expect it was that
d d squatter,” replied Bill.
“ Hullo ! ” said the overseer, catching sight of
the dead sheep ; “ how did that happen ? ”
“ My dog killed them. Which of your men
broke down our fence last night ?”
“I know nothing about breaking your fence
down ; but you’ll have to pay for those sheep.”
“ I’ll see you in h—l first.”
“You’ll go to gaol, then.”
“ Will I ? You mangy squatter’s cur ! Get !”
Tom stooped quickly, picked up a clod, and
threw it with all his strength. It struck the over-
seer’s horse on the neck, making it rear so vio-
lently as to nearly throw him. Then the horse
bolted.
“I guess he had to get that time without wish-
ing it, ” said Bill.
Three days later Tom was arrested for sheep-
stealing. Bill offered bail ; it was refused. The
squatter was the magistrate; and Tom got three
months’ hard.
Bill was in a fearful state, and felt sorry Tom
hadn’t shot the squatter when he came to the hut,
and taken to the bush. “ And people wonder how
bushrangers are' made,” he thought. On his way
from the court-house, a man spoke to him whom
he had never seen before.
“ Didn’t you fellows notice that no land was
taken up on the run ?”
“Yes.”
“ Do you know why ?”
“ No.”
“ Oh ; I see you don’t know the squatter’s repu-
tation, then. Look here, young fellow, you’d
better take good advice and get !”
“I’ll be hanged if I do.” And Bill walked on.
Three weeks after, the crops were up again,
when it struck Bill he had better keep watch ; so
he slung Ins hammock between the trees on an
uncleared part of the selection, close to the cleared.
The third night he fell asleep, and got a good
ducking from an unexpected shower —which, how-
ever, did not wash away his intention, although it
made him use powerful bush English. The fifth
night he was awakened by the bleating of a
frightened sheep. He was up like a shot; but a
glance showed him that the mischief was already
done. Bill was just about mad—all the madder
that it was partly with himself for falling asleep
again.
The men driving the sheep did not know they
were discovered. They knew, first, when they
saw their horses galloping wildly away ; and,
secondly, when one of them was tumbled over by a
chunk of wood, senseless ; for Bill properly recog-
nised that one man was easier to fight than two.
Then the fun began. Both were over six feet,
wiry, and in splendid condition; but Bill felt the
moral superiority of the injured party. Round
and round they went, just like a pair of game
cocks, feinting and plugging, but neither getting
home properly. At last Bill planted one between
the eyes, which knocked his opponent clean off his
feet as he stepped back to avoid it. Up he got,
and sailed in again. Bill already felt sure of vic-
tory, when he got one on the side of the head
which made him see stars.
They parted at this, for both felt a bit dazed,
and sat down under the trees about twelve feet
apart, eyeing one another with interest.
“ You can fight,” said Bill.
“ So can you.”
“ You’re pretty game,” continued Bill.
“ Not gamer than you, anyhow.”
A solemn silence now intervened, only inter-
rupted by a satirical chuckle from a jackass
perched on a bare branch above them, the hoot of
a distant mopoke, the night wind gently rustling
the leaves, and the quick breathing of the two
men.
They rose simultaneously ; again they faced.
The fun became faster and more furious, for
neither intended to fight another round. Bill was
more careful this time, giving no opportunities and
taking all. At last Bill saw his man show signs
of temper, and commence to let out savagely. He
immediately became more wary. His opportunity
soon came ; one on the nose and a right-hander on
the point of the jaw, and the squatter’s tool lay
stretched out without an ounce of fight left in
him.
• # •
At the end of his term Tom was liberated;
but both he and Bill were different men.
Whilst Tom was in gaol, Bill was harassed
by every means ingenious malice could sug-
gest. So they determined to clear out, and
to take every chance of getting even with
the squatter. Their horses were harnessed to the
drays ; utilities were packed into them ; the hut
was knocked to pieces ; and everything that might
be useful to the squatter, which they couldn’t take
away, was destroyed. The moon and a few night-
birds were the only witnesses of their departure,
which was as they wished. They felt a little
regret at leaving what they had intended to make
happy homes, but wore too angry for the feeling to
last long. They went direct to Albury.
* # #
The next day the squatter had to call out
all his men and ask his neighbors’ help to quell the
fire that had broken out on his run. Most of the
grass on the station was destroyed before the fire was
extinguished, and he had to sell half his sheep at a
loss for fear of losing all by hunger. All who
knew the story of the selectors chuckled, and said
they had calculated well and begun early.
The squatter had runs in the three eastern pro-
vinces to the number of eight. And it was now
observed that Regularly once a year some misfor-
tune would happen one of them ; until it was said
by other squatters that Joe Jackson’s luck had
turned. This run of misfortune lasted several
years, and was making the squatter’s hair turn
white. Tom and Bill occasionally saw him with the
worried look on his face ; it made them turn away
and look as if they had just listened to a good
joke. Then the bad luck suddenly ceased,
and the squatter’s stations gave their usual re-
turns —all except one, into the dams of which a
lot of Murray crabs had found their way; which
was ingenious.
* • •
Bill grew tired of wandering round the
country ; so he looked out for a quiet job as shep-
herd or boundary-rider. He found a billet which
combined the two on Joe Jackson’s station in
Victoria, where no horse could be used on account
of the rough nature of the country. This station
was in the great bend of the mountains where the
systems of N.S.W. and Victoria meet. Beyond
the boundary of the station it was considered too
scrubby even for rabbits. Bill had not been on
the place six months before he knew better.
The summer had been very dry; the sheep were
thin and inclined to wander. One day, on going
his rounds, Bill found the fence broken down and
about a thousand sheep missing. The other side
of the fence was a strip of bare rocks for about
half-a-mile ; beyond, impenetrable scrub.
Bill mended the fence and reported the loss,
with details, to the super.; who only said:
“ Queer! we lost a big mob some time ago. Any-
how, old Skinflint will be glad ; it will save him
the expense of boiling down —which doesn’t pay—
and let the rest have more grass. He would have
killed them for manure if he hadn’t feared the
5.P.0.A.”
Bill returned to his hut and books—for Bill had
taken to improving his good State-school educa-
tion—and thought no more about the matter for a
few days. Then he began to wonder where such
a big flock could have got to. By reading, he
knew the country beyond was practically unex-
plored ; and his knowledge of sheep told him that
if there were no grass they would have come back
again. Then he came to the conclusion that they
were either dead or had found food.
Bill’s dog was a very good one, as was proved
by the many attempts to steal it. Bill took
it to where the sheep had broken through, and
said, “Fetch ’em out, Jacky !? Jacky crossed
the stony patch, straight for the scrub, and dis-
appeared under it. Bill followed, dived under the
scrub on his hands and knees ; worming his way
with difficulty, and getting many a whack from
back-swinging branches. After 30 or 40 yards of
this he noticed the scrub thinning and becoming
taller, until he was able to walk upright. This
thin band was about 15 yards wide, being due to
an outcrop of rock. On each side the scrub was
as dense as ever.
About six miles were passed thus, the scrub
becoming small trees as he ascended. Then Bill
passed over a ridge through a gap, which, as he
descended, became a ravine, and dropped very
abruptly into one of the prettiest valleys imagin-
able, with the lost sheep grazing as contentedly
as pigs in a potato-patch. He had in sight about
10,000 acres, and about 5000 sheep, the valley
being sparsely timbered, well-watered, and capable
of feeding about seven times as many sheep.
That night, on his return, Bill wrote a letter to
Tom, telling of the find, and saying he intended
using the sheep, with Tom’s help, for their mutual
benefit, since they both owed old Jackson a grudge,
and this was one of the best ways to pay him out.
Tom read the letter with interest; then sadly
shook his head. The cause of this sadness was
Tom’s religious turn. A travelling parson had
besought him to alter his sinful ways, and to
become generally godly —that is, like the parson.
Tom, of a more pliable nature than Bill, listened,
and was converted. His answer to the letter ran
thus;—
■» My Dear Brother William,— l am very much Grieved
to think that you have not yet altered your Sinful way of
living’. I feel sure you would do so, if you could only see
and hear such a man as Mr. Snuffle ; he is a most Godly
man, and has made me feel that I would like to be Good.
The Good Book says that we should forgive one another ;
and I have forgiven Mr. Jaokson. Do so, too, dear
William, and let us not take another man’s Sheep.—From
your loving and anxious brother, Thomas.
, Bill looked very queer after reading this, gazing
absently into the flames. Then he took up his
pen and wrote :
Dear Tom, — Go to Blazes. —Yours as ever, Bill.
When Tom got this he looked very queer, too,
and thought hard for a bit. Then he kicked the
parson’s tracts into the fire; and the parson enter-
ing at the moment, Tom felt inclined to kick
him—but didn’t. In a month Bill and Tom were
together again.
They worked the find in perfect secrecy. Tom
acted as drover, going into N.S.W. to bring down
sheep for the Victorian markets, and mixing their
own drafts with them. They also improved their
sheep by borrowing some first-class rams from
Jackson, returning them before they were missed.
Two years after, they had saved up a good, round
sum.
The sheep had begun to wear a track at the
valley exit, which made Tom and Bill fear it
would be discovered; so Bill went to the Lands
Office and applied for 20,000 acres in his own
name and Tom’s.
Stingy Jackson, as he was called, was not a
little surprised that one of his boundary men had
applied for land so near his—and with such a bad
reputation, too. But as Bill had, in Jackson’s
eyes, become a squatter, he called on him and
asked to be shown over the place. The request
was'granted; he looked at the sheep; then he
looked again.
“ Where the devil did you get those sheep ?”
“ Found ’em,” said Bill.
‘ ‘ Why, damn it, man, they must be what I lost
two years ago !”
“ I think they are,” said Bill, with deliberate
vagueness, “ but they may not be.”
Jackson saw he could not prove the sheep were
his in a law-court; but told everyone the new
fellows were thieves, and had admitted the sheep
were his when he recognised them. Tom and Bill
denied it, and were more generally believed ; for
mean men like Jackson do queer things some-
times.
Jackson then took to relieving his feelings by
calling them scoundrels, liars, and so on. Tom
met him in the township one day and threatened
to hammer him. Jackson slunk away cursing,
and repeated his slanders. The day after Bill met
him in the township pub., and belted him soundly.
Jackson sued for assault and battery; at the
trial, the whole history of Tom and Bill came out
(with some exceptions here and there)—their
selections, Tom’s gaoling, and who gaoled him.
The result was—“ Verdict for the defendant.”
Stingy Jackson slunk out of the courthouse,
amid baas and caterwauls, like a beaten dog.
Bill afterwards got into Parliament, and sup-
ported all the good land legislation brought in.
And when men selected on his and Jackson’s
stations he helped them—particularly those on
Jackson’s. Bill was one of the few men who can
stand success.
W. BRENTA.


THE GHOSTS OF THE DESERT
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I had ridden far over the country side,
Where all things living had drooped and died ;
Where the soil was blasted, the land accurst,
Its veins licked dry by the sun’s fierce thirst.
The plains stretched on in an endless maze,
And over them danced a shimmering haze—
A freakish haze that in glamourie
Turned a stone to a rock, a bush to a tree.
The blacks had told us—they lie at best—
Of a fair lagoon in the unknown West,
And I rode through the blaze of a summer day
To find out the spot where that water lay.
Timber in sight! the blowsy sun
Dropped suddenly down, for his work was done.
Timber it was ! I looked around —
The withered blue-bush lay on the ground.
Through the sullen twilight ! rode ahead
Till the stalks of the ’lignum shone gaunt and red ;
But the hole was dry, the bed was bare,
The bones of a wild dog rotted there.
The dash of a thunderstorm had passed
O’er one patch of ground and that spot was grassed
With short burnt feed, and I blessed the sight
For my tired horses would feed that night.
I had food, in my bag was some water lees,
So I camped by a thicket of mulga trees,
Made a fire of the scrub-wood hard and dead,
On the saddle rested my weary head.
Into the circle of light and flame
A fluttering figure with bright eyes came—
Came, and perched on a naked tree —
An owl; and it solemnly stared at me.
Over the tree-tops the sky grew bright,
The great round moon rose into sight
Rose with a sick and yellow gleam
Like the dim, dead moon of a nightmare dream.
“Ha ! ha! ” cried the owl. “Ho!ho ! ” cried he,
And the moon and the owl both stared at me.
I slept as tired men sleep, and the pain
Of the long, hot ride was dreamt o’er again.
Then I startled woke—on my ear there fell
The sound of a deep cathedral bell.
The night was clear, the owl sat there ;
The scrub loomed dark, the ground .was bare ;
Afar on the plain a shadowy pile
Rose in my sight ’neath the moon’s cold smile.
Dreaming or waking, I cannot tell,
But I followed the voice of that deep-toned bell.
“Ha! ha!” cried the owl. “Ho! ho !” cried he;
But I left him perched on the withered tree.
There in the heart of that desert land
I saw a towering minster stand,
Its rich-hued windows ablaze with light
Whilst pealing music awoke the night.
At the open portal I stayed my tread
For a moment—then entered and bared my head
In my dust-stained garments amid thai host
The only one who was not a ghost.
Though the drooping faces were wrung with pdn,
I felt no fear in that haunted fane,
But I saw that my presence, unbidden, strange,
Wrought swiftly a sid and sudden change.
The altar lights burned dull and dim,
The choir’s loud chant sank low,
Instead of the grand triumphant
hymn,’
That had made the air to throb and
swim,
’Twas a requiem sad and slow.
Strange were the forms who knelt in
prayer,
For the dead of the desert were
gathered there;
Whites—with the thirst and hunger
brand—
Uplifted to heaven a fleshless
hand.
Blacks—with a bullet-shattered
bone—
Uttered a long, reproachful moan —
All who had fallen, by scrub and
plain,
From the eastern coast to the western
main.
All who had suffered fell Nature’s
wrath
In the van of a nation’s onward
path,
Had died in their harness, unknown,
unwept,
That night in the desert a vigil
kept.
Though their white lips moved not a
breath they drew,
The deep responses giving,
As I looked at their cold fixed eyes I
knew
That dead souls prayed for the living.
And still as I listened there seemed to grow
A cry of anguish, a wail of woe,
From that spectral throng, and they sobbed in
P ain ,
For the lives laid down and all in vain.
They had dreamt, as their eyes closed dull and
They had opened a track for a nation’s tread ;
They had thought, as the failing brain grew weak,
That smiling plenty would line each creek ;
That the toiling poor would enjoy the lands
They had fought for and held with their dying
hands;
That farm and vineyard would grow apace,
Where they looked their last on a lifeless space.
But the western downs, where the red sun sank,
Are held in the name of a tottering
bank;
And the boundless pastures, the ver-
dant sod,
Belong to an oily man of God.
The cities, where vice and crime cry
loud,
Are rearing a stunted and pallid
crowd ;
And the taciturn bushman of sturdy
stamp
Gives place to the loafing, foul-
mouthed tramp.
And they moaned - uplifting their
hollow eyes—
“We were offered a useless sacri-
fice ;
For greed and plunder our blood was
spent—
For the curse of a rotten Parlia-
ment.”
And ever and ever, in gathering
swarms,
Came trooping and trooping those
awful forms,
Till the din of their wailing grew
loud and higher,
And I woke, with a start, by my own
camp-fire.
In the west the burnt-out moon hung
low,
The scrub-wood ashes were white as
snow,
The owl still blinked, with a drowsy
stare.
And the morning star was a glory there.
The grey sky blushed with the dawning red.
At the first faint gleam the phantoms fled ;
The owl flew off, w ith a mocking scream,
And daylight banished that wild, weird dream.
ERNEST FAVENC.
The " Woman Scorned."
A TRAGEDY OF THREE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Stuitzen, pacing to and fro upon his post,
peered anxiously through the darkness towards
the prison building —a long, black blur upon a
starless sky. With every minute’s delay that
lessened his chance of the reward, the risk he ran
appeared to dwindle —the reward itself to swell;
but when, at last, two women’s figures, black and
noiseless, took sudden shape beside him, the
danger reared itself again before his mind in full
proportions.
“ As silent as death,” he whispered in the taller
woman’s ear. “Listen!”
From right and left came distinctly through
the stillness the rhythmic steps of men—like
Stuitzen, water-sentries —but not like Stuitzen,
expectant of 12,000 guilders for contriving the
escape of a Sumatran “princess;” which per-
sonage—the shorter of the women—made now a
gesture of impatience, said something, also, in a
voice so loud that, on the instant, the distant
pacing ceased.
“ Down with her to the boat, quick!” the warder
whispered, rapidly, “ but touch nothing till I
come”—and lie resumed his measured tread, until
his fellows, judging all was right, did likewise.
“ Whites—with the thirst and hunger brand —
Uplifted to heaven a fleshless hand.”
Then Stuitzen spat contemptuously towards the
prison, unfixed his bayonet, slung his rifle, and
, ■ cautiously down the shelving sand-bank.
T B women were already in the boat, and the
Ui iU pushed her off with ease, for here, inside the
Isle of graves, there was bub little surf. Little
wind, either—for the same season—and slowly
they stood over for Chapel Point. Outside,
against the sea-wall of the prison-cemetery, the
waves would make noise enough to drown their
voices—but they couldn’t weather the point, and
unwillingly Stuitzen put the boat about for a short
board to south-east —a shift of helm without which
this story would never have been written. lor the
“ princess ” —understanding only that the boat
was heading back towards Padak —sprang up with
a scream that cleft the night-air for a mile. A red
flash answered from the shore —then, with the
sharp bark of the rifle, came a fainter flash from
“ But the hole was dry, the bed was bare.
The bones of a wild dog rotted there.”
further off —a duller report—another, and another
—as the alarm circled completely round the island.
“Smother the she-devil!” hissed Stuitzen to his
wife; and better would it have been for Mrs.
Stuitzen —late female warder in Padak —if she
had literally obeyed his order. But only with
handkerchief and hand she stopped the other
woman’s mouth, and held her till the boat, upon
her former tack, was standing out to sea. A
silent boat, then, for the Sumatran had collapsed
in the bows, and the Stuitzens were listening for
something. Two minutes, and it came —the
cannon-shot that carried far and wide the news of
a prisoner’s escape, and loosed upon the startled
night a sullen roll of corroborative echoes.
Then, again, silence, and Stuitzen —his dis-
jointed utterance travelling with his slowly-work-
ing wits—said, “That —settles it! No use, now
—to run for the Tidambang ! The kanonneerboot
will be there before us ! We’ll have to make for
the Carabongs, and keep dark till the fuss is
over!”
» • •
“ Six weeks we have been here !” said
Mrs. Stuitzen, answering her husband. “And”
—with a significant glanoe at the Sumatran—“it
is high time for us to go !”
The man laughed uneasily. Marital infidelity—
conventionally attributed to innate depravity—is
just as often due to nothing worse than the
craving of variety, and Stuitzen —a moral non-
descript, with brutal possibilities—might never,
under ordinary circumstances, have discovered
even that he wanted a change. But he had his
full share of vanity, and the knowledge that
Ganga—this buxom, brown woman with the
splendid eyes—was striving to supplant his wife,
set him noticing how hard Mrs. Stuitzen’s Frisian
features were growing—how thin her sandy hair,
and how dim her pale-blue eyes.
He thought it best to bluster. His wife-he
told her with many Dutch expletives was a
jealous idiot! “ Bonder en weerlichtl —did she
think he was never going to look at another
woman? And, in short, he was a man, and would
do as he chose !”
“Not this, Sybrandt!—after all these years !”
and the hollow-eyed woman caught his unwilling
hand.
Ganga laughed—the poisoned snigger with
which woman stings woman. “He is mine !’’ the
laugh said. “ Poor wretch ! —you can do nothing
with him, now I”
Hekla Stuitzen —a woman of the people—had
no conventional dignity to sustain; she stepped
swiftly across and struck the “princess” on the
mouth—a blow that started blood out of the full,
insolent lips. Stuitzen’s bayonet lay in a corner
the tent, and the Sumatran —a triple murderess
already—had it in her hand before the man could
interpose. But the Dutchwoman —officially ac-
customed to emergencies—parried the thrust with
one hand, wrested the weapon away with the
other, and sent it whizzing out over the rollers
into deep water.
Stuitzen threw off the mask. Taking Ganga
round the waist, he drew her back, kissed the
blood from her mouth, and looked round savagely
at his wife.
“ Your place is outside, now !” he said. “See
that you stay there! ” and, without a word, she
went.
• * •
With night came a heavy westerly blow,
but the fringing reef took most of the surf, and
the tent—well stayed with boat-gear—stood the
squall. Biit outside, in the rain and darkness,
Mrs. Stuitzen sat and tried the occupants for
their lives. Where folk were numerous, she
thought — where she might have had a chance of
seeming not to know —it might have been more
endurable. But here —the only three human
beings within a hundred miles —the insolent
grossness of the thing was intolerable. “ The
woman shall die 1 ” she decided —“and the man,
perhaps I ”
* • *
Sfcuitzen came from the tent and called.
Upon the reef the snowy ridges were still thunder-
ing in, but, beyond, the sea shone blue again to
an unclouded morning sun.
Mrs. Stuitzen went wearily over—a piteous
figure, with sodden hair and drenched garments—
and her husband, pipe in mouth, let a shadow of
compunction steal into his florid, wooden face.
He brought out a little metal cup of brandy and
held it towards her. She drank it, for she didn’t
want to throw away her life—yet.
“ Well, vrow ! ’’—Stuitzen avoided her eye—
“you see what a fool you’ve been, by this time, X
hope. You can come into the tent again, now,
if you like ? ”
“ Call me no longer vrow ! ” she answered
quietly. “And, if I ever enter that tent while
the is in it, I shall be as mean as—as you are !”
Stuitzen dashed his pipe in her face. “ Verdoem-
nisi—stay out, then! And, look here !—make
yourself useful!’’ He brought out half-a-dozen
turtle’s eggs. “ Make a fire and roast these for
her breakfast ’’ —pointing back into the tent.
But no such petty malice had power to touch
this apathetic woman. She calmly took the eggs
and did as she was bidden. ‘Then while the others
ate, she wandered off along the beach till Stuitzen
called her back. He was struggling with laughter
and a mouthful of egg. “ Ganga says,” he began
—and broke out into a fresh guffaw.
“Ganga says,’’ took up the Sumatran rapidly
in Malay, “ that if it be thy purpose to drown thy-
self, better would it be to do it here, that Ganga’s
eyes may be gladdened with the sight.”
“And this,’’ said Mrs. Stuitzen, in Dutch, “is
what this creature laughs at!—this heartless hog I
have so long mistaken for a man!” Then, in
Malay—“ Myself I will nob drown, woman.
Drown me, thou, if thou darest! —thou or thy
miserable .”
Stuitzen stopped her speech with a will-aimed
egg—an act more farcical than tragic, but an act
that sealed his fate. The outraged woman made
no sign. Submissively she cleared the stuff from
her face—submissively endured the other woman’s
taunting laughter. “He will be going to the hill
to look-out presently,” Mrs. Stuitzen told herself ;
“ and he will be afraid to leave her here with
me !”
She was right. Her husband brought out his
rifle and a shot-gun. “ Better out of your way,
these playthings !” he said to the white woman ;
then to the brown one—“ Come thou with me,
Ganga! If no sign I see to-day of that accursed
kanonneerboot, from here will we sail to-morrow !
With favourable winds three days should see us
in thy river of Tidambang.’’
“ Willi her?” said Ganga’s questioning glance.
Stuitzeu’s shaken head answered “No 1” —and
Stuitzen’s wife, understanding, hardened her
heart the more.
• • #
She could see them through the trees—
black specks upon the low basaltic ridge that
made the island’s northern end. But they could
not see her, she knew.
She went into the tent and brought out a
canister of gunpowder, provided by Stuitzen, the
methodical, for the service of the shot-gun. Off
this she took the lid, and, raking away the embers
of the fire, buried the canister so slightly in the
sandy soil that its open mouth was barely screened
from view. Waiting, then, until the embers were
extinct, she gathered them again into place above
the hidden powder.
“I will hide ! This snake of a woman will have
to cook. Then ! ”
For 20 hours she had not eaten, but thirst was
all she felt, as she stole down into the hollow
where the sago-palms were thickest. There was
water here, in pools amongst the stiff, red clay.
Mrs. Stuitzen drank, and lay down to wait,
*» , •
“ lb may be that she has drowned herself,
after all.”
“Not she!” the Dutchman answered, angrily.
“ She may do evil to herself after she has done it
to us—not before. She will come back when she
is hungry —as lam now. There are more eggs in
the tent. Fetch them, my princess, and light the
fire. And be careful of these; they don’t grow on
trees hereabouts !’’
He kissed the Sumatran as she took the matches
he held out, bub the other—seeing all from her
hiding-place—only smiled. “She won’t be so
kissable in a quarter-of-an-hour—if all goes well !”
And the white woman crept a little nearer.
The fire burned slowly up. Ganga put down
the eggs to roast, and Stuitzen came over to light
his pipe. Carelessly he stooped, resting one hand
on the brown shoulder of the kneeling woman ;
then, suddenly—where both had been—there was
only a cumulus of dense, sulphurous smoke a-°,
with a thump that shook the ground the watcher
lay on, the canister exploded ! Then, shriek on
shriek made music to her ear, as she ran up and
saw, rolling on the ground, a dreadful, hairless
object, with its blackened hands pressed to its
blasted features. Bub the Dutchman, with his
clothes on fire, lay still enough !
# • *
A Banca fishing-crew, poking about
amongst the less-known Carabongs, came cue day
on a spirit, stalking by a lonely shore. Tiny
thought it was, at all events, and were getting out
their sweeps in a mighty hurry, when the thing
cried out to them—in Dutch, one said. “ Ami
who,” he wanted to know, “had ever heard of i
Dutch antu ? ” He’d swim ashore and speak t>
it—this daring fellow—if the others were afraid.
They were ; and he did ; and in the end the craft
took on board a white woman —gaunt and wild-
eyed and ragged—who was Mrs. Stuitzen.
She went with them to Banca, and there —being
a wise woman—held her peace until her death was
at hand. Then she told.
“My husband never stirred. There was apiece
of the canister so firmly fixed in his forehead that
I couldn’t pull it out. And, badly as he had
treated me, I was glad he hadn’t to suffer like the
other. She lived three days—and then I was
alone. Sorry? No—it was justice! I would do
it again I*

A MORE OR LESS MERRY CHRISTMAS.


M'Fee
of aberdeen
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They’ve scraped her sides, and tarred her ropes, and
patched her suit o’ sails,
They’ve filled her full o’ varied stock for firms in
New South Wales ;
She’s left her berth on London Docks, she’s left
the Lizard light,
Ajid in the rough Atlantic now her bowsprit stabs
the night.
But, rough or smooth,
or foul or fair, what-
e’er the waters be,
He’ll take her out and
bring her home or
sink her, will
M‘Fee.
They’ve seen the sun
go down, go down,
and turn her canvas
red, ’ 1
And as she rides the
darkened seas
they’ll watch the
stars o’erhead;
They’ll watch thestars
that splash the skies
with sparkling sil-
ver spray, ...
Out in the Great Un-
fathomed Deep
away, and still
away !
But when the Trades
have filled her sheets
and sing among her
shrouds,
Like some gl.ad, buoy-
ant, spirit thing
she’ll leap towards
the clouds.
From mom to noon,
from noon to night,
she’ll pitch and roll
and toss,
And as the Bear goes
out o’ sight they’ll
see the Southern
Cross;
Across the Line and
off the land, hull-
down this side the
Cape,
By chart and com-
pass, and the sun,
her outward course
he’ll shape;
And be the ocean deep
and blue, or be the
ocean green, .
’Twill not affect his wonted calm —M‘Fee o’
Aberdeen !
The Glasgie skipper, homeward-bound, will pass
him on the way,
And as she dips her colors aft his crew will hip-
hooray,
For in the ports where sailors meet and out across
the sea
Hath passed' the name and gone the fame o’ sturdy
Jock M‘Fee.
Though print has spread and wars have raged
and rebels have been hung,
Though o’er and o’er the world has changed since
Jock M‘Fee was young,
The ways of steam he will not learn ; but, Lord,
to hear him speak
Of racing trips and rousing deeds when ships were
built o’ teak !
Ere paddle-wheels or double-screws had altered
all the years,
And sailor-men were sailor-men, not “ sea-sick
engineers.”
So build your oieamboats big as towns, electric
lights and all,
By wood and canvas, to the end, McFee will
stand or fall;
For wood and canvas, wind and tide, the books of
sky and sea,
With strange salt oaths and curses make the
knowledge of McFee.
The wars may come, the wars may end and crowns
be lost or won—
He rolls around the rolling world that rolls around
the sun.
And men may write most wondrous books, and
men may count the stars,
His aim in life is still to get all sail upon his spars,
Nor does he care how kings may fare or empires
may decline,
When, underneath his vessel’s keel, deep lies the
cable-line;
But skies of lead and seas of ink, when winds like
devils roar,
Will find her reefed and taut and snug, bare poles
and well off shore.
Some fingers, mate, are made for pens, but they
be white and soft,
And some are made as hard as nails for clewing
sail aloft,
E'er short’ning sail on stormy nights, when
the w t wind takes your breath;
For holding fast to greasy yards when letting go
means death I
So in his log-book, “ out and in,” no flowing lines
you’ll see,
But scrawling entries, short and curt, hard-written
by M‘Fee.
Said entries treat, in sailor terms, of how the
Betsy, barque,
“Was met in” —longitude exact —“May 25, at
dark,”
Or “ Sighted land at 10 a.m,” with soundings such
and such,
Or “Smith, A. 8., from crosstrees fell,” or
“Passed screw-steamer, Dutch.”
Aye, round the world, and round the world,
where’er his owners will,
His cargo aft to land and leave, his for’ard hold to
fill;
Across the seas, and o’er the seas, and o’er the
seas again,
Through night and morning, clear or cloud )
through calm and wind and rain,
She’ll roll along, she’ll pitch along, she’ll tack, and
turn, and drive,
NEW CHUM (to station-overseer): “My — er — man, are theah any—ah—
serpents about heah?"
STATION OVERSEER : “ Well no, there ain't no serpents to speak of, but there's
some snakes here plenty big enough to swaller the like of you whole
and never know they'd had anything for breakfast; an' if that ain't
good enough, yer better hurry back to London, where I dessay
yer'll find some in the Zoological Gardens as'll suit your eddycated
tastes."
And while her spars still in her stand she’ll come
to port alive.
But if her “ sticks ” and she should part, and jury-
masts should fail,
’Tis said M‘Fee would doff his shirt and still
contrive to sail. ;
The port is not on charts laid down, nor put on
maps, I ween,
Where, in his youth, or in his prime, some time he
hath not been.
He’ll talk and tell of Plymouth town, of far
Alaskan bays ;
Of New Orleans and Puget Sound, Colombo, and
its ways ;
Of arrack drunks, and sam-shu sprees, of Old
Kaintucky rye;
But when he comes to talk of girls, be sure that
none are by,
For sailormen are sailormen; the same right all
the way
From Glasgow to the Golden Gate, from Rio to
Bombay.
And Neptune rules the rolling deep, but Yenus
reigns ashore,
Sorest assured that Yenus is—as Venus was of
yore !
A lusty glass of smoky Scotch, and pass the cabin
jar ;
Here, fill yer pipe with “duty free,” and smell
the smell o’ tar.
Oh, hear ’em tramp the planks above, “ Ey-hey !
they strain and creak —
The music of the blocks, my lads, ’tis good to hear
them speak.
But, oh, the sough o’ swirling seas that from her
glide and go, , „ .
The song of lone, mid-ocean winds, and all the
songs ye know;
So roll along, so race along, so tack and turn and
drive,
You’ll get a taste o’ sand and weed, or else —come
back alive ;
You’ll take a swim some stormy night, but not for
pleasure’s sake,
Or else, in ninety days from now, a deep, long beer
you’ll take !
So pull away and haul away, and let the chanty
rise—
’Tis watch and watch for ninety days and nights,
and “damn yer eyes.”
’Tis watch and watch when on the poop your
skipper takes his stand ;
When far behind and low behind and out o’ sight
the land !
“ Sou’ east by east ” her course is set, and “ nor’
by east ” again,
With every inch o’ canvas on, she cuts the seas
amain,
Across the world and round the world and bits o’
port between,
He lives the life that sailors live, M‘Fee of
Aberdeen.
Sydney.
EDWIN J. BRADY.
"INDIFFERENT
HONEST!"
ASTUDY IN RACES
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
When, for 60,000 dollars, the Sultan of
Johore parted with Singapore Island, Rizah Ben
Abri became —much against his will—a British
subject. A good many years ago that had been,
but, the more Rizah saw of the “ noble Anglo-
Saxon race,” the less he liked it. A Singapore-
born Arab, Rizah, as became a faithful Islamite,
was temperate, just, inexorably upright; whereas,
in the average Briton of the Straits, he saw but
a creature wrapped up in its gross material needs.
That such a guzzling, swilling, money-grubbing
animal should have been allowed so extensively to
replenish the earth, was to Rizah but another
proof of the utter inscrutability of Allah’s pur-
poses.
“ Sends He not forth also the locust and the
pestilence ? ” thought Rizah, as he stepped out
upon the platform of his house and looked away
into the blackness. Not altogether blackness,
either, for, though the starless, sweltering night
showed nothing of the Johore jungles, down
amongst the mangroves of the hither shore the
swarming fire-flies flashed out, all together,
twenty times a minute, a white, electric pulse
of light that brought each twisted bush for
an instant clearly into outline. Idly Rizah
watched a sight not new to him, till presently,
amidst the lovely evanescent gleaming, there stood
out, large and steady, an eye of coarser yellow
light. Indoors went Rizah for a kris, then stole
down his ladder and made cautiously for the man-
groves. “Let me see now what is here ?” he said
to himself. “No Chinese thieves do I want so
close to my borders !”
It was dead low-water, and right and left the
night-crabs scurried out of Rizah’s way as he
picked his steps among the arching roots. The
fire-flies were taking now their periodic rest, but
all the brighter through the ragged boughs shone
the yellow light of the lantern. For a lantern it
was, making a little island of glare upon the stink-
ing mud, naked here of mangroves for a space of
20 yards across. In the centre of this opening
stood a white man, coatless and hatless—big,
burly, and perspiring. Beside him lay a chunkal
—the Chinese spade-hoe—and at his feet a little
surface of blacker mud accounted for his moisture.
He had been digging in the mud and filling up the
hole again.
“Something buried!” thought the’Arab. “I
will wait!”
Into the filled-up space the white man now
stuck, nearly to the head, an iron skewer, from
which, successively, in three directions he paced
the distance to the nearest bush. Then, from the
pocket of a flannel jacket—hanging on a branch—
he took a note-book, and, stooping with it to the
if.
“ I was as close to you as now I am! ”
the Arab said, and with the words the other's
weakness passed; the dangerous animal awoke
suddenly within him. He shut the door, backed
up against it, and thought—Rizah read it in his
cruel mouth —of homicide l
light, brought his face into clear illumination. A
full-fed countenance it was ; fleshy of nose, heavy
of jowl, and trimly iron-grey of whisker.
“ This face shall I remember well!” said Rizah
to himself ; then softly he followed the other, as,
having hidden his chunkal, donned his jacket, and
picked up his lantern, he made down towards the
water. Out went his light when he reached it,
but by the resumed gleaming of the fire-flies Rizah
saw him take the sculls of a dingy and pull away
along the shore to the eastward.
“Hardly will he have rowed round here from
the city,” thought the Arab. “ Perhaps from
Franklin Point he comes ; he looks like a man of
substance and repute. But, nevertheless, with
morning light will X see what here lies buried !
* * *
Amongst the Cosmopolitan Christians —
that will do for the name—there was consternation.
To the O.C.’s —besides a nucleus of Europeans—
belonged Eurasians and Bengalis, Chinamen and
Javanis, all of them devout and many of them
wealthy. And of this olla podrida of a Church
the comer - stone was the Reverend Anderson
Sweetman —saint, shepherd, and treasurer ! But
now from the holy man’s snug nest at Franklin
Point had been looted in the night his strong-box
with the year’s subscriptions and, so he said,
every penny he himself possessed. “ The China-
men again !” was the cry, and even the Chinese
“organ” lent its pipe to the weary old Press-
fantasia about the uselessness of the police, who,
for their part —having oracularly pronounced
“ Chinese gang-robbers ! ’’—were apparently in-
capable of saying, or doing, any more.
Meanwhile the Reverend Sweetman, having—
before the Sanhedrim, of the Cosmopolitans—
deplored, even unto tears, his Christian trustfulness
in not banking the money, had received plenary
absolution, topped off with a consolatory subscrip-
tion. All which things, having duly filtered, per
medium of the Malay-Arabie newspaper, into
Rizah Ben Abri’s cognisance, so moved that
dusky moralist that he sought out and studied a
photograph of the Cosmopolitan pastor,
“ That is the man ! ” said Rizah then. “ Justice
shall he do ! or to Justice will I deliver him up ! ”
* * *
“ These infidels make their Paradise on
earth ! ” said the Arab, as he stood next morning
in the porch of “ Gilead.’ Shut in from the outer
world by a hedge of bamboo and wild heliotrope,
the Saint’s retreat presented a picture of night-
freshened tropic beauty with which the holy man
himself —pig-eyed and gross-featured—discorded
painfully, as with a crimson cummerbund about
his abounding belly, he came to the door and
stared insolently at his visitor. No Arabs had he
in his flock; no need, therefore, to restrain his
native boorishness.
‘‘ In private ? ” he echoed, with a dissatisfied
grunt. “ Come in here, then, and be quick ! ”
* * *
“ What cock-and-bull story is this, my
good fellow ? You say you know where the money
is ! Then you must know who took it ? ”
“It is so ! ” returned the Arab, coolly. “ I
know who buried the box among the mangroves
at Tanjong Rangon ! ”
Bead-drops—not from the heat —started suddenly
out upon the shepherd’s unwholesome brow.
“ Tanjong Rangon !” he repeated slowly ; then
in a husky whisper—“ You saw me ? ”
“ I was as close to you as now I am !” the Arab
said, and with the words the other’s weakness
passed ; the dangerous animal awoke furiously
within him. He shut the door, backed up against
it, and thought—Rizah read it in his cruel mouth
—of homicide !
“And he has three times my strength!” the
Arab told himself. “Fool that I was, to come
unarmed !”
But there was no need of arms. The servants
had seen the fellow, Sweetman reflected, and—in
short, it wouldn’t do! He locked the door, put
the key in his pocket, sought inspiration from a
“ peg ” —and found it. The sullen jaw relaxed ;
the murderous eye grew calm.
“ Well, then, Rizah Ben Abri—if that’s your
name —what do you mean to do ?”
“It is you who have to do something, not I.
Restore this money! Act like the man of God
you are —supposed to be !” and over Rizah’s
thin features passed the
shadow of a sneer.

door, went out, and gave

heard him confess that he stole the box and has it

In Sangarook Asylum—sent there from
prison—there is a cranky Arab who maunders
day and night about a man of God, a money-box,
and a mangrove-swamp. A genii'-, inoffensive,
old man—he suddenly forfeited ot - day his repu-
tation for harmlessness and went tooth-and-nail
for one of a party of visitors.
“ Poor creature !” said the assaulted one, when
the lunatic had been dragged off. “ I remember
him now! It was myself he robbed, but, as he
confessed his crime, I did all 1 could for him. No
use, though—it was a flagrant case, and an example
was urgently needed.
The “poor creature” is Rizah Ben Abri—
enjoying the reward of virtue !
