AN IDTV OF
NURRA
MURRA
"COLONIAL EXPERIENCE."
(FOR THE BULLETIN).
He was twenty five ; had a big nose, a long name,
and a very large idea of himself.
He could say “ Djah boy” and “gude bai”
with the most approved English accent. He wore
checks so big that you were always wondering
whose move it was next.
His under-jaw protruded slightly, and the lower
teeth came a little forward over the upper, and
made him lisp when he summed up the people of
Nurra Murra as human monstrosities. He was
clean-shaven and tall. And he served eggs and
calico, rice and elastic, behind the counter of
Nurra Murra Freetrade stores.
He gave you to understand that he was not
born serving eggs and calico. He never said so
directly, but he made you know it subtly, and you
were ashamed of doubting.
The verdict of male Nurra Murra, long with-
held and scantily expressed, was a unanimous one
when it did come out. It was that John Cotting-
ham Everard Major was a born gentleman. With
the accent on the born.
The people of NurraMurrawerenomore addicted
than those of other country towns to making free
with the store assistants. Yet the C.P.S. handed
him his pouch when he met him at the reading-
room at nights ; the doctor yarned openly to him
in Nurra Murra High-street; the parson invited
him to drop in some night; the bank-manager was
civil.
The bank-clerks alone sneered loudly and kept
aloof. And Charlie Pearson was neutral; though
perhaps inclined to the bank-clerks’ views.
It was Charlie Pearson’s wife who first set the
ball rolling towards J.C.E.M.’s feet.
“I don’t care,” she said, “counter-jumper or
not, his manners are perfect.”
“Damned cheek,” said her husband. “What
have counter-jumpers to do with manners?”
“Or road-superintendents!” said she. Which
included him, and was cruel.
He winced. Then he smiled.
“Or their wives !” he said and, sitting down to
the piano, began the melody of “ She’s a Nice
Little Innocent Thing.”
“And I shall bow to him whenever I meet him
—there now !” ended Mrs. Pearson.
Everybody knew the story of Mrs. Pearson.
Her brother had been manager of the B.J.W.
Bank, at Nurra Murra, and she had come from
Melbourne to stay with him. She had just left
school then. And she had met Dick Thomson
there, and had loved him, even as he loved her—
or said he did. He had thrown her over, though,
for another girl who could pay his debts, and she
had married Charlie Pearson straight off. He
had asked her because he loved her, and she had
squared her brows and put a smile upon her
mouth, and stuffed Pearson into the gaping hole
the other man had left in her heart.
It did not answer, though. There came a time
when Pearson left off calling her darling, and
stopped asking her to kiss him. He was kind to
her always—a little rough sometimes; he took as
much trouble as ever to tiptoe when his boots
creaked, and to fetch her eau-de-cologne when her
head ached, but he left off going with her to
church, and took to spending his odd hours at the
doctor’s diggings. There was always somebody
to talk to there. As the doctor said, the Nurra
Murra folk were beastly healthy.
Mrs. Pearson hated, despised, and was deadly
tired of Nurra Murra.
“God made it last of all,” she wrote to her
favourite brother, a Melbourne QC. “A stick-
in-the-mud pig-sty. The men talk sheep and
wool, read ‘Rolf Boldrewood ’ and the Nurra
Murra Advertiser. The women talk fowls and
cooking and read nothing. Mrs. James, the
parson’s wife, who represents the intellect of the
place, reads a serial now and then in the weekly
paper. She doesn’t mind that, she says. It gives
you time to think between each issue. And I—
-1 forsooth, I can find nothing else in all the world
to do but think. I have left off asking them here,
but on Mondays, when I’m supposed to be at
home, they all flock round for afternoon tea—
here’s a specimen :
Me : How d’you do, Mrs. Green. Very hot,
isn’t it ?
She [gushingly) : Yes, isn't it. (Subsides sud-
denly).
Mb : I shall be so glad when the winter
comes. Do you take sugar?
She : Yes, pleaae.
Me : The summer’s so trying.
She; Yes, tsn’iit?
Me : Did you go to the tennis yesterday.
They had very good play.
She : Yes, didn't they ?
Me : I love tennis. So good for the com-
plexion.
She : Yes, isn’t it?
Me ; Not if it’s too hot, of course.
She ; No.
Me ( desperately ) : I read a good deal in this
weather. Just finished “ The Light that Failed.”
Have you read it ?
She : No, I haven’t.
Me : My brothers send me books from Mel-
bourne. They sent me “ Barrack Room Ballads ”
to-day.
She : Did they ?
Me : Do you care for poetry ?
She : When it’s read aloud. John reads aloud
nicely.
Me ( when she has gone) : Damn it! (I learnt
that from Charlie.) . . .
Nurra Murra ladies, in bedroom confidences,
called Mrs. Pearson headstrong. They would
have liked t_ make it “fast,” but she snubbed
their men so mercilessly that they couldn’t
do it.
One evening she went over to the library to
change her books. She herself had got up the
theatricals that had placed those books upon the
shelves. And she had chosen many of the books,
and was the bane of the council clerk’s life,
always giving him orders and advice, writing
lists and trying vainly to keep him up to date.
She came in listlessly this night. She wore a
white dress and a great hat that hid the childish
beauty of her face. Her books were in her hands.
She laid them on the counter,
“Good night, Small.”

LOUISE M. MACK.


NIGHT IN WARD
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Night in a hospital ward begins at 8 p.m.
—the hour at which performances commence in
the theatres, and the world outside the hospital
walls begins to “spend the evening.” To the
patient who thinks himself well enough to be out
of bed for another couple of hours, at least, this is
a fact full of malignant irony.
There were many such patients in surgical
Ward Z. when I was staying there. One of them,
particularly, a young Hebrew tobacconist, who
was beginning to feel his own man again, after a
grim struggle with the Enemy, could not conceal
his contempt for a rule which, to use his own
words, compelled grown men to go to bed at the
same hour as children.
He would stand at the window, near his own
bed, looking out wistfully at the lights in the city,
and noting aloud the passage of time by the Post-
office clock.
“ Quarter to eight,” he would say; “just time
for a cigarette and a drink before turning into one
of the theatres.”
“ Ten to eight—the trams are coming in loaded
with playgoers.”
A long pause, punctuated with heavy sighs.
“ Eight!”
The lights in the ward would be turned out, one
by one.
“ The curtain is just going up—and I am going
to bed in the dark. Urr ! ”
And he would undress himself, cursing like
Shimei, the son of Gera.
“ You ought ter be mighty glad, young feller, to
have a bed to get into, these times, ” the Old Sun-
downer would remark, when he heard him, in a
tone of grave reproof.
# # *
The Old Sundowner made no secret,
amongst the other patients, of the fact that he
y was in no hurry to vacate his bed; indeed, he gave
a striking proof of his views on the matter before
I left the ward. He was practising, one morning,
with crutches on the floor of the ward. His leg
had been put in plaster-of-paris a day or two pre-
viously, and he knew that this was a sign that he
would have to go forth into the cold world again
as soon as he was able to move about without
assistance. There was no gladness in his eyes
because of this knowledge. He was not pining
for freedom. And, all of a sudden—the floor being
very slippery—he fell down and broke his leg
again. Whereby he keeps possession of his bed
until the hard winter is over and the wattle blooms
in the land, and a careful man may camp out once
more without fear of rheumatism.
“ P’r’aps,” said the jockey-boy, when the young
Hebrew tobacconist made us more than ordinarily
tired with his yearnings after theatres, “p’r’aps
one of these days you’ll be able to go to the theatre
without leaving the hospital—and be the star of
the performance, too.”
The jockey-boy referred to the Operating
Theatre,
* * *
The operating-theatre, I may explain, is
an amphitheatre in miniature. In the centre of
the arena stands a long table, beside which,
weapon in hand, the Gladiator of the occasion
takes his place. Upon the table lies the Christian
—I mean the patient. He is meek, uncomplain-
ing, resigned, chloroformed. On the benches
around, tier over tier, sit the vulture-eyed, shark-
lipped Novices of the Knife.
“I am now,” says the performing Gladiator,
addressing them, “about to show you how at one
swoop to cut out the, say,, corpus callosum.”
. Hp, plunges his weapon into the back of the head
of the victim. A quick turn of the wrist —and
ms hand flashes in the air, with the corpus callosum
held triumphantly between the thumb and fore-
finger.
“ Hagc / ” cry the embryo Gladiators, the young
Sawyers, on the benches above.
This is a, specimen of the entertainments held in
the operating-theatre.
* * #
The comic-singer was another of the
patients in Ward Z. who chafed at the Curfew
rule. His body was in the hospital, but his soul
was in the Victoria Hall. His bed was next to
mine, and sometimes we used to talk together in
whispers after the lights were turned out.
I’ve danced my last jig—which,” with melan-
choly humour, he said to me on one of these occa-
sions, “was a breakdown,” (It appeared that he
had broken his leg, in a transport of terpsichorean
frenzy, on the benefit night of one of his particular
chums in the “ Amalgamation.”) “ But I’d give a
lot to see the other boys shaking their legs just
now.” And then, perhaps, he would speculate as
to what he should do when he left the hospital.
I think,” he would say, musingly, “ when they’re
putting my leg in plaster. I’ll ask them, while
they re about it, to put me in plaster altogether, or
up to the neck, at any rate. I might then be able
to make a sort of living as the Singing Statue or
the Melodious Marble Man in the Waxworks.”
# i. # #
None of the patients I have referred to,
except, possibly, the young Hebrew tobacconist,
really wanted to be out of the ward after eight
o’clock. Their trouble was that, at that time,
they were left in the dark, with nothing but their
own thoughts to occupy their minds. If there
had been enough light by which to read for
another hour or two they would have been satis-
fied. Anybody who has for years made a practice
of reading himself to sleep will recognise at once
the serious nature of their grievance. When a
man who is compelled to remain in bed is not per-
mitted to read, and is unable to sleep, and is
forbidden to sing, or otherwise make a disturb-
ance, what is there left for him to do but lie
awake and think ? And thinking—in a hospital
bed, at any rate —means thinking about the past.
* * *
One begins by trying to call up pleasant
recollections of the good and generous deeds he
has done during the course of his life. He
manages to while away in doing this a space of
time varying from five minutes to a quarter of an
hour, according to the amount of virtue there is,
or has been, in him.
My memories of this kind used to last a full
quarter of an hour—but, then, I used to think of
them twice over, slowly.
And then he lies awake till the small hours of
the morning, writhing as he thinks about all
the mean and wicked things he has done, and
most likely falls asleep before he has got half
through the list of them, and dreams that a
Hantean devil, with a terrible trident in his hand,
is turning him over on the inconsumable anthra-
cite in order that he may be properly browned
on his other side.
He awakes, gurgling and gasping, to find that
the night-nurse is shaking his shoulder and telling
him to make haste, and wash his face, also his
hands, while it is yet dark.
Tor such is the custom in Ward Z—and, I sup-
pose, in other wards of other hospitals. The
night-nurse has to begin thus early in order to get
through her work in time.
As far as work and responsibility are concerned,
I would rather, if I had to take the choice, be three
or, at the very least, two day-nurses than one
night-nurse.
* * #
On a night I have in mind, ward Z was
more than usually quiet. The theatrical tobac-
conist was silent. Pie was going back to his beloved
theatres and gas-lit streets and cigarettes and
drinks on the following morning.
Tor nearly an hour 1 amused myself drearily by
looking at the night-nurse writing by the light of
her solitary candle. I think she was transcribing
“ The face of the man described as the new case.”
notes on lectures which she had attended, but she
may have been writing a love-letter. They
become accustomed, these nurses, to doing either
thing with equal gravity, after a time.
Then the doctor came in and he and the nurse
talked together in low tones about a case which
had been brought in two or three hours previously.
I knew all there was to know about the case—
a word of horrible impersonality which makes the
flesh creep even as the dreadful term “ It ” makes
the blood turn cold.
The man had been carried in on a stretcher by
two hospital assistants. He looked 40 years old,
or thereabouts, and wore a set scowl on his sallow
face. He had black hair, black, intelligent eyes,
and might have been pleasant to look upon when
in health, but, as it was, the dogged and bitter
curl of his thin lips repelled sympathy, even in
thought. One could see from his expression that,
if he had to die, he had made up his mind to
die as the wolf dies —biting.
He was a Oornishman, a very ordinary Cornish'
man, not the best type of Oornishman by any
means ; and yet it was curious and strange to note
how, under the supreme stimulant of suffering,
the grossness disappeared from his face, and his
profile came out clearly and finely as a profile upon
a Punic or Phoenician coin. As well it might, if
we may believe the Cornish antiquarians—Borlase
and others.
TTis wife, a thin, little woman with a drawn,
anxious face, accompanied him, and stayed after
he had been put to bed, stayed with him until the
doctors came. Then she had to go, though much
against her will.
* * *
A dozen or more of these black-coated
gentlemen—of whom it behoves me and all others
who have come out of their hands alive, to
speak with respect —surrounded his bed. They
were all there, from the great surgeon, firm-
mouthed, keen-eyed, square-chinned, and with a
massive head which looked as if it had been cut
out of granite, to the young “house-doctor,”
respectfully supercilious, who sees always, in his
mind’s eye, a passage from some future history of
medicine, commencing, “ This astounding, yet
simple revolution in the practice of medicine and
surgery was inaugurated by Q. X. Smith. He
was the Harvey and Jenner of his time. He was
the apostle of the new epoch. ”
They are, generally speaking, very clever fellows,
these house doctors, but they sometimes make one
think of what Job —that ancient, patient Job—said
to his friends, "No doubt but ye are the people,
and wisdom shall die with you.”
The result of the consultation, which occupied
less than five minutes, was that the new case was
taken down to the operating theatre, from which
he was not brought back for over an hour.
He was then placed in bed again, and the
ominous dark red screen was drawn around the
bed.
The nurse sat down beside him.
# * *
Suddenly from the special ward—a small
room, containing two beds, situated at the further
end of the main ward—the crash of a falling body
was heard (by the nurse and myself, at any rate),
followed immediately by a furious pounding on
the floor. “Jack” was the owner of the body;
“ Old Dad ” was the pounder on the floor.
, _ “Jack” was a burly young man who had been
injured in the head by the bursting of a boiler.
“ Old Dad ” was laid up with a broken leg which
prevented him from moving with celerity, and
also suffered from some affection of the throat
which kept him from raising his voice above a
hoarse whisper. The direst part of the complica-
tion —to “ Old Dad ” and the night nurse—was sup-
plied by ‘ ‘ Old Dad ” himself. Nothing could rid him
of the belief that “Jack” —who certainly had a
curious craze for falling out of his own bed in the
dead of night and rolling over the floor in the
direction of “Old Dad’s” bed —cherished a furtive
but implacable desire to murder him.
A faint wake of light along the ceiling marked
the progress of the nurse, candle in hand, down
the ward.
“Jack ” was put to bed again, and all was quiet
once more.
* * •
And the hours of the night went on.
The moon in her course passed from window to
window down the long northern side of the ward.
And as she passed she threw a broad band of
lustre on each bed in the line of her light. There
were two faces she touched which she would never
touch again—the face of the poor, little, flippant,
light-hearted jockey-boy, and the face of the man
who was described as “ the new-case.”
The clock struck four and the nurse arose
softly from her chair and began to make the
beds. This bed-making is a simple operation
as conducted in the hospital. The nurse slips off
the quilt, puts a clean sheet in its place, draws the
(upper) soiled sheet from under the clean one, and
replaces the quilt. Voild tout I The patient is
not uncovered for a moment during the process.
The withdrawing of the under sheet is quite as
simple and modest an operation, but need not be
described.
I heard a sound of growling, in a cracked fal-
setto brogue, from a bed occupied by an old
countryman of my own who had, I believe, been
brought ixxto the hospital on the day previous,
suffering from soxne slight injuries obtained
through being run over by, I fancy, a hearse. And
the following was something like the conversation
which took place between him and the nurse :
“Now let be, young ’ooman, let be.”
“But I must do my duty.”
“I’m ould enough to be yer grandfather, ye
hussy, and I won’t allow ye to dhrag the clothes
atf me.”
“ I’m not going to drag them off you if you- .”
“Let be, 1 tell you agin. It’ll be less throuble
to you, and less thrial to me if you do. I’m ould,
and I’m cowld, and I won’t have the clothes
dhragged aff me—so ye can put that in yer pipe
and shmoke it, me gyurl.”
The nurse at this juncture remarked that if he
would not allow her to make the bed quietly she
would report him to the doctor.
“ Och, is it the doother ?” he said, with pitying
irony. And then, addressixxg the ward, and the
world outside, and all created space, he repeated
in a tone of shrill sarcasm, “ I report yez to the
docther, she sez ! Report me !, Why” (dropping
his voice to the tone of one who might be address-
ing a corpse, which he despised, buried in a deep
grave), “if ye say another word,me gyurl, I’ll re-
port ye ! The docther knows. me, let me tell ye„
He was house-docther whin 1 was here beforo-ih
this very same hospital, twinty-siven years/age.
What do ye say to that, now ?” r j / T
The doctor of whom the poor old man was,
speaking had been dead over terx|years. The nurse
whispered in his ear (so she told me afterwards)
that there was a patient dying in a bed lower down
the ward, and the old man was as docile as a doll
in a moment.
* * *
The chill air preluding the dawn came
through the window of the ward. The nurse was
by the side of the dyixxg man. I suppose that she
and I were the only persons in the ward, or in the
world, who knew that he was dying.
He died—it is a dreadful thing to say, but it is
true—cursing and blaspheming, and yet in posses-
sion of his senses, so far as could be judged by his
comprehension of things around him.
The nurse asked him if he had a message to
leave for his wife.
“ Tell her to go to H—l, where I’m going,” he
replied.
I had heard of such death-beds before, but was
never before, and never desire to be again, so near
to one of the kind as I was then. I remember the
case of a woman who was regarded ,as a saint by
the members of her sect, and by herself and her
family as a mother in Israel, and by all outside as
a good and pure woman. She died, with all her
family and friends gathered around her, stating
that she was in her right mind, making careful
disposition of her property—and utterly abjuring
not only all that she had professed (and wxthout
hypocrisy) to believe in, but openly regretting that
she had lived a virtuous life, and usxng language
too hoxribly vile to be conceivable as coming from
the mouth of a woman—least of all a dying
woman.
* * *
In the grey dawn two men, clad in white
canvas suits, came into the ward carxying between
them something that looked like a very long
trunk.
They stopped beside the bed surrounded by the
dark-red screen. The lid of the trunk was lifted,
there was a slight noise behind the screen, the
trunk was closed again, and the men walked away
with it. The screen was then folded up and put
aside. The bed was empty.
* # #
When a man dies in his own bed, in his
own house, with weeping friends gathered around
him, death takes the form of a solemn and almost
singular visitation. A lonely death in the bush,
under the sky, with no man near, is, as an idea,
full of tragic majesty. But tlxere is neither
solemnity nor majesty about death in a hospital.
It is a matter of business, of ordinary routine —as
is, of course, natural and to be expected. One
goes to a hospital either to get well or die —and to
do one thing or the other with as little delay as
possible, because other people are waiting to be
served.
The wife, now a widow, of the new case came
to the ward early in the morning to ask about her
husband. The nurse told her how it was with
him. She trembled a little, and hid her face in
her hands for a moment, but took the blow bravely,
like the brave little woman she was.
“Did he —did he leave any message for me?”
she enquired with a break in her voice.
The nurse looked her squarely in tlxe eyes and
lied nobly. “Yes,” she said, “he died praying
that God would take care of you.”
And it was day once more in Ward Z.
V. J. DALEY.
That Swag.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The Western train had just arrived at Redfern
Railway-station with a lot of ordinary passengers
and one swagxnan.
He was short, and stout, and bow-legged, and
freckled, and sandy. He had red hair and small,
twinkling, grey eyes, and—what often goes with
such things—the expression of a born comedian.
He was dressed in a ragged, well-washed print
shirt, an old black waistcoat with a calico-back;
a pair of cloudy moleskins —patched at the knees
and held up by a plaited greenhide belt buckled
loosely round his hips ; a pair of well-worn, fuzzy
blucher boots, and a soft felt hat, green with age,
and with no brim worth mentioning, and no crown
to speak of. He swung a swag on to the platform,
shouldered it, reached out a billy and water-bag ;
and then went to see about getting a sheep-dog out
of the dog-box.
Five minutes later he appeared on the edge of
the cab-platform, with an anxious-looking cattle-
dog crouching against his legs, and one end of the
chain in his hand. He eased down the swag
against a post, turned his face to the city, tilted
his hat forward, and scratched the well-developed
back of his head with a little finger. He seemed
undecided which track to take.
“ Cab, sir !”
The swagman turned slowly and regarded
“ cabby ” with a quiet sort of grin.
“ Now, do I look as if I want a cab ?”
“ Well, why not? No harm, anyway—l thought
you might want a cab.”
Swaggy scratched his head, reflectively.
“Well,” he said, “you’re the first man that
thought so these ten years. What should I want
a cab for ?”
“ To go where you’re going, of course.”
“ Do I look knocked up ?”
“ I didn’t say you did.”
“ And I didn’t say you said I did. . . . Now,
I’ve been on the track this five yeai's. I’ve tramped
two thousan’ miles since last Chris’raas, and I don’t
see why I can’t tramp the last mile. Do you
think my old dog wants a cab ?”
The dog shivered and whimpered ; he seemed
to want to get away from the crowd.
“But, then, you see, you ain’t going to carry
that swag through the streets, are you ? ” asked
the cabman.
“Why not ? Who’ll stop me ? There ain’t ho
law agixx it, I b’lieve?”
“ But, then, you see, it don’t look well, you
know.”
“ Ah ! I thought we’d get to it at last.”
The “ trav’ler ” up-ended his bluey against his
knee, gave it an affectionate pat, and then
straightened himself up and looked fixedly at the
cabman.
“Now, look here !” he said, sternly and impres-
sively, “can you see anything wrong with that old
swag o’ mine ? ”
It was a stout, dumpy swag, with a red blanket
outside, patched with blue, and the edge of a blue
blanket showed in the inner rings at the end. The
swag might have been cleaner; it might have been
newer; it might have been hooped with decent
stx-aps, instead of bits of clothes-line and green-
hide —but otherwise there was nothing the mattei
with it- as swags go.
“I’ve huinped that old swag for years,” con-
tinued the bushman; “ I’ve carried that old swag
thousands of- miles —as that old dog knows—an’
no one ever bothered about ‘ the look of it ’—or ol
me —or of my old dog, neither ; and do you think
I’m going to be ashaxned of that old swag, for :•
cabby or anyone else ? Do you think I’m going U
study anybody’s feelings ? No one ever studied
mine ! I’m in two minds to summon you for usir.v
insulting language towards me I”
He lifted the swag by the twisted towel which
served for a shoulder-strap, swung it into the cab
got in himself, and Hauled the dog after him. ‘
“You can drive me sdmewhere where I can leave
my swag and dog while 1 ! get some decent clothe*
to see a tailor in,” he said to the cabman. “My
old dog ain’t used to cabs, you see.”
Then he added, reflectively: “I drove a cal
myself, once, for five years in Sydney; ”
HENRY LAWSON.


Larry .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The world, I know, is still as fair
As ever it has been ;
As blithe in Springtime is the air 5
The grass is still as green ;
The sun shines bright as e’er it shone;
All pleasant things abide :
But half my joy in life is gone,
Since Larry Doran died.
With early friends who die our youth
Dies, too, and each young dream.
Thus unto most of us, in sooth,
Dead friends the dearest seem.
In evTy graveyard, the world o’er,
Each white memorial stone
Marks the last resting-place of more
Than, ah, the dead alone.
A gipsy in a
Was Larry, rest his soul!
But city ways he held in scorn.
And civilised control:
As through the veins of Primal Man,
Through his ? in rhythmic flood,
To lilting, roving measure ran
The old nomadic blood.
His was the gay philosophy
That sunshine and sweet air
Their lovers teach, and his the free
Bohemian scorn of care.
His, too, the Wit that springs to birth
By social hearths at night;
As playful as the flames its mirth,
Its hues as rich and bright.
The bravest, simplest, best of souls
Was he ; a bearded child—
Too fond, perhaps, of flowing bowls
And midnight wassail wild;
Great-hearted as the day, from marge
To marge of its sky-wall—
The marvel was a heart so large
Should be so light withal.
When, blue as mom, the lilac blooms
Broke forth from purple bud,
And young gums lifted crimson plumes,
Like spear-blades dipped in blood,
And from each graceful pepper-tree
The coral beads did hang;
Then gaily, swag on shoulder, he
The song of roving sang.
His dream was Riches to amass,
And he, in mining phrase,
The diggings followed, but, alas,
In later, poorer days.
Erom Palmer, in the hot Ear North,
To Snowy River cold
He roamed; the gold his luck brought forth
Was ever fairy gold.
No man more sanguine could you find
Beneath the blue sky’s cope;
Pandora’s Box was, to his mind,
A sea-chest packed with hope.
Each new-found Gully, fresh-struck Elat,
Bathed in his fancy’s glow,
Became another Ballarat,
Or else a Bendigo.
And I went with him, once upon
A time —long past, ah me !
How gaily on that morning shone
The sun ! how gay were we !
With swags strapped tight, and pipes alight,
And billy-cans m hand—
There were, that mom, few spirits bright
As ours in all the land.
The merry heart that goes all day
Was his : through shine and shade,
Like sunbeams glancing through the gray-
Green leaves his fancies played.
Of yams and jests, and songs and glees,
He had a wondrous store ;
Sure, laughter large as ours the trees
Had never heard before !
The kookaburra, keen of ken,
The wild wag of the wood,
Would gravely listen, now and then,
As though he understood.
And, 10, the feather’d humorist rich,
Of tail all innocent,
Would shake with laughter, like to
which
A giant might give vent!
Thus gaily all day long we
tramped,
And, when the night drew
near,
Then by a burning 1
encamped
How merry was our
cheer !
And ever, in the hush
profound,
When Larry chose to
sing,
The dim, mysterious
Bush around
Seemed darkly listen-
ing.
When sundowners by
chance we met,
No word at first we
spoke-
The true sundowner’s
etiquette,
A mute and solemn
joke.
But when our swags were
on the ground,
Ourselves the swags
beside,
We calmly filled our pipes all
round,
And gravely smoked —and lied.
What cared we for the world that drive#
Poor souls to madness then ?
What for the cares that fill the lives
Of city-prisoned men ?
I pass them by, these men, each day ;
I hear their fetters clink ;
The world’s work must be done, they say—
’Tis done too much, I think.
The city is the Home of Greed,
Where Mammon has his throne;
And some on hearts of others feed,
And more upon their own.
The friends that once I used td meet
Therein, no more I see :
The very stones that pave the street
Like gravestones seem to me.
I hold it true that in that hour
When cherished Dreams depart,
And Life’s, onoe-honeyed wine grows sour,
And callous grows the heart,
And confidence gives place to doubt—
’Tie better to be dead—
And Larry Doran died without
A grey hair in his head.
There is a star that twinkles through
The air at evenglow :
I think it is the world whereto
Gay souls like Larry’s go.
And, by good fortune, I may see
Him there, or—who shall say ?
May meet him roving carelessly
Along the Milky Way.
C.R.

GANCY M'LAY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“No! Football ain’t what it used to be. You
can’t touch a man now without ’aving a free-kick
give agin yer, though I ain’t played meself now
for this six year. I got jack when we was done
by the Star—the Star o’ the Flat, y’know. I
belonged to the Oyster Bay United. The Star
was a push yer never could trust; they was as
artful as a ’atful of monkeys, and yer couldn’t
guess what their lay was. The fust match with
’em, we ’ad ’em fairly done; dead to the world,
they was, at the end o* the third quarter, and
their followers ’ad their tongues ’anging out like
’sif they was dying. They only ’ad one man
among ’em that was worth a place—that was
Pippo O’Shea. I never seen his like on a football
field, an’ I kid meself I know a bit about the
game. ’E was only a thin slab of a bloke, not
more’n ten stone, but, my oath, he could play.
Talk about glue on yer ’ands, why, do me, the
ball used ter foller ’im like a little dawg. ’E was
everywhere at once, ’e’d run and dodge like a
grey’oun’, and I never seen anyone in the same
street’s ’im at a ’igh mark. If Pippo was alive
now he could knock three quid a week outer any
senior club for a monte. He died in the ’orspital
’bout a year ago. Too much colonial.
“Whatyer say? ’Bout the match? Oh, yes!
We was level in goals, but we ’ad ’em under the
whip, and they saw it was a blue duck unless

seeing, and when the ball come near ’im ’e roosted

a bit too cunning for us all. I got a ’old of ’im by
the neck just near our goal and slung ’im jus., as ’e
rose for a mark. It was a toss tliat’d ’a’ broken
nine men’s necks outer ten, but ’e turned as ’e come
down and fell on ’is ’ands and knees as right as
rain. Then ’e grinned at me and ’eld out ’is ’ands
for the free kick as confident as could be, and
punted the ball through’s if ’e’d just come on the
ground. Seven to four they kicked up to the
middle o’ the last quarter, then I see it was all up
with us and I give our barrackers the orfice and
the scrappin’ started. But we got the worst o’
that, too, for Pat M'Carthy took their part. D’ye
see that mark on my jaw? That’s where he got
a straight left ’orne. I can feel it still when the
cold weather starts.
“ And d’ye know how they pinned us ? It
makes me wet now when I think o’ it. They
dropped to the dodge o’ putting Gancy in the
team (I think some o’ our blokes must a’ been
skitin’ about the way ’e was going to put Pippo)
and they faked up a scrap between ’im and one o’
their barrackers as ’e was coming down to the
match. The feller says to Gancy, ‘So yer goin’
ter play for the United. D’ye think ye’ll win?’
‘My oath!’ says Gancy, ‘we’ll do ’em bad.’
‘ They can’t ’ave much o’ a team though,’ says the
bloke, beginning ’is funny business, ‘or you
wouldn’t get a game.’ This maddened Gancy,
for ’e rather fancied ’is carcase as a footballer,
though, as I told yer, he couldn’t play for apples,
so’e says, ‘That’s what you think, is it? Well,
there’s some things I can do if I can’t play foot-
ball.’ ‘P’raps,” says the bloke, ‘But fightin’
ain’t one of’em.’ So Gancy plugs ’im and they
goes at it ’ell for leather in Smith-street. Of
course, it didn’t last long and a couple o’ copmen
soon run ’em both in. Then Gancy seen through
the game and ’e tried to get ’em to let ’im go
but not them, so like a blooming fool ’e started
tlirowin’ off at ’em, so when he wanted them to
’unt up bail for ’im yer can betcher life they
didn’t burst their ’arts chasing round,
“It was a mean, contempable dodge for the
Star ter work off on a club what’d alius treated
’em fair, and who only wanted to play a ’onest,
manly game and let the best side win, and it fairly
disgusted me with football, straight, it did. It’s
gettin’ low, I thinks, and so I tossed it up. But
it was good while it lasted; it’s a fine game still;
but it ain’t what it used to be, not by a long ways,
So long. Gerrup.—Milkaieough 1 ”
YARKAMAN.
Westward Ho!
(FROM THE FIREMAN’S POINT OF VIEW.)
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It may seem all bright an’ joyful to you chaps in
the saloon
Who are feedin’ on the knowledge you’ll reach
Hannan’s pretty soon,
And the sooperfloous deck ’ands, an’ the cursin’
swearin’ mate,
An’ the squad o’ tucker-jerkers—which them
stewards all I ’ate—
But us firemen raisin’ ’ell right from the Schanck
to Moreton Bay,
Gettin’ racin’ run from this old tub to make the
dam line pay—
We’re ancient cocks at thirty, an’ our pay’s re-
duced to seven,
Though when we got nine quid a month this
wastin’ job was Y eaven—
Oh, ’twas ’eaven for us clinkers,
An’ in those times we were drinkers ;
But now with baker’s f am’lies an’ with two
quid off our draw—
When the skipper rings the bell,
An’ we fire our floatin’ hell,
We start eight red-hot watches for but grub an*
kids ashore.
An’ the owners an’ their union who ’ave got on us
the grip,
An’ brought us down to seven a month —why !
we’d give a bloomin’ trip
For every hour they'd do below a-sweatin’ like the
sea
When it strikes this crawlin’ tub an’ bursts in
drops upon her lee ;
We’d like to see ’em sweatin’ double tides to win
a race,
An’ the second engineer a-bullyin’ them to make
the pace;
Or to see ’em even greasin’, with their bellies
black an’ stark,
A-gropin’ for the bearin’s in the smotherin’, oily
dark —
Oh, we’d like to see ’em there,
When the mate begins ter swear
At us clinkers emptyin’
windward ashes
blown again on
deck ;
Oh, they’d sicken
of the smell
Of this rank an’
floatin’ hell,
An’ they’d let the
hooker founder an’
become a blasted
wreck.
An’ yer try to sink yer
sorrers in a dozen
quarts o’ beer,
An’ the heat in thirty
seconds makes yer
''* head an’ eyesight
clear—
You ain’t even got the
opshun of a good
an’ ’olesome booze,
For the fire leaps out
an’ dips you in a
clammy, weakenin’
ooze.
An’ the owners curse the captain for not makin’
faster trips,
An’ he curses at the engineer, who comes straight
below an’ rips
All the blue an’ stiflin’ curses he was ever taught
or told
At us wretches breathin’ sobs, an’ swelterin’ in the
molten hold;
An’ we curses at the trimmer,
An’ we ’opes his eyes ’ull simmer
In the deep eternal stokehole where the watches
never go—
An’ the trimmer blasts the coal
Havin’ no inferior soul
For to curse at in the Crowd of graded despots
down below.
Oh ! they’re right enough an’ rumbo, are the sailor-
men on deck,
For they never do a ’and’s turn but to keep the
ship from wreck;
An’ they scrub the rails like slaveys, an’ you hear
the skipper swear
When you put some coal-dust on that rail a-comin’
up for air;
An’ they ’ave the women on the bridge with a
blind man at the wheel—
Which that self-same flirtin’ on the bridge ’as
crippled many a keel-*
An’you hear the mate say, “Yes, Miss, a good
run—the wind is aft,”
An’ us clinkers dyin’ cause there ain’t a breath to
help our graft;
For when the wind’s ahead,
Up on deck the talk is red;
But our ventilators take it all, an’ we've an easy
go ;
But a wind that’s right abaft
Calls for double graft an’ craft,
An’ it’s ’eaven to you salooners, an’ it’s hell to us
below.
So you boys go round the Leeuwin for a country
growin’ gold ?
An’ we’re sweatin’ ’ere at thirty, an’ we’re blood-
less, an’ we’re old,
With but the little hope that all our coal won’t be
half slate
Which we fired with in the strike-time for to keep
our sailin’ date.
We used ter hope some angel chap ’ud smash the
engine gauge
That’s a tell-tale on our firin’, .but we’ve lost the
hope with age;
But you’re better off, an’ younger, an’ you’re sure
to strike a few,
While we aged young ’uns stay below to drive this
hooker’s screw.
An’, maybe, when we’ve a hearse,
The engines ’ull reverse,
An* our ghosts ’ull stay on deck an’ join the cabin
mess an’ so—
While the owners who are dead
Till pray for wind ahead,
A-firin’ up for ever in the hungry hell below.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.


WHY NOT?
(FOR THE BULLETIN .)
Do you remember, old fellow, our parting
some time ago at Singapore, when we went our
different ways, to East and to West, to barbarism
and to civilisation, each bound on the same errand ?
We were full up then of Charters Towers and of
Gympie, had been cleaned out at Cloncurry, but
two months on the blazing hills at Croydon had
given us our fares and a little over, and there we
were. Once a gold-hunter, always a gold-hunter.
Sustained by the hope that springs eternal in the
prospector’s breast, you were bound for payable
leads in the territory of the Sultan of Pahang,
while I was travelling West to see if the man who
had floated the Great Ebor Gold Mining Co.—
capital .£5OOO, no liability—could ruffle it with
the men of London city, and perchance place a
mine or two with a public over-anxious to grow
rich.
Since then our paths have been always diverging
and our quest has been the same. The gold was
not in Pahang, but the fever was ; and a sceptical
British public, 44 got at ” once or twice, would not
look at my mines, though I had brought them rich
specimens in a sack. We keep hoping all the
time. You may be hobbling your horse and
making your fire on a flat concealing its thousands,
and I may awake to-morrow in this dingy, dirty
London to find a multitude clamouring for sound,
speculative mining investments. Who knows
when the patch may be struck or the boom begin?
But, however that may be, we shall at least have
lived our life out and not have droned it away.
We parted ■ . th i P. and 0. boat Hector, home-
ward bound.
Do you re-
member the
day ? A blaz-
ing hot sun
(how I long for
it now), a sky
of one dead
blue, and the
city white and
dusty an d
crowded in'
the distance.
Seated on the
after-deck we
smoked our
kst smoke to-
gether and
watched the
passengers
tanglingthem-
s elves and
their friends
into a state of
confusion.
S ingapo re
merchants,
agitated mo-
thers, little
children, na-
tive nurses,
Malay porters
and Chinese
traders, fare-
wells, wel-
comes, objurgations and hysterics. What a
babel! and why, oh why, do most people
make such fools of themselves at such
moments? We watched all this for a time,
and then—our attention was drawn to the woman
in grey. It was the contrast, probably, that first
attracted our notice, fbr, like ourselves, she was
unmoved by the turmoil. Seated on a deck-chair,
quietly reading, or watching the passers to and
fro with a lazy, half-contemptuous smile, what did
we see ? Height, one- would say, about sft. 6in.
(rather tall for a woman), large dark eyes, color-
less face, expressive mouth, figure good but
slender ; age, perhaps, 24 ; dress, grey, cool and per-
fect. Maid, wife or widow, we wondered —and
then our speculations were broken by the parting
bell, and you went your way and I went mine.
Of all that has happened since, you must wait to
hear till we meet again ; but I cannot forbear to
tell you a little now. Life on board an ocean
steamer, who can endure, at the time, with
patience, or think of afterwards without regret ?
After all, there are only three things to be done.
Some come out with heavy volumes and a pretence
of work —they are only frauds seeking an excuse
for sleep; other few harry the unoffending with
suggestions of ship’s cricket or quoits, or such like
inanities; but the wise treat them with contempt.
The life is so placid and monotonous that the
strong man must have one of the strong passions.
He must gamble, drinkj make love, or fight like a
berserker with some idiot for whom he has con-
ceived an uncontrollable hatred. As you know,
I am little or nothing of a ladies’ man. It is not my
line. Besides, have I not a wife somewhere, who
knocked all that nonsense out of me long ago ?
However, I can play most games of cards not a
little, and watch the moon come over the foretop
with any man who ever paced a deck (there were
not many who could see us through a long night
in the good old times), and so the days passed not
unpleasantly. The little smoking - room was
always full, the long 44 pegs” went down well in
the hot morning, and the short ones equally well
in the cooler evening, while we were making for
Colombo.
One night, after dinner, I sat down on deck
next to the woman in grey. I had not been able to
help hearing about her. Name, Mrs. Sabine, the
wife of a year and a widow 12 months, returning
to England to live ; undoubtedly wealthy and cer-
tainly sufficiently attractive to cause her sex to
make ill-natured remarks. I had noticed her
often, but we had never spoken more than a pass-
ing word—and yet I had watched her a little.
Always the same, calm and impassable, always
exhaling the same faint odour of opoponax,
usually seated on the most comfortable chair in
the most comfortable corner of the deck, reading
or talking. She treated the two or three young men
who had constituted themselves her admirers with
a suavity and indifference which would not have
flattered them had they been older —a cold woman,
one would have said, casually looking on, but
those large, dark eyes betrayed her. The eyes are
the windows of a woman’s soul, and it was looking
into those that I thought I saw daylight—or was
it night ?
As I sat down that evening I had not noticed at
first who was next to me. We were at the far end
of the deck, and the moon, like a disc of gold, was
only just rising from the edge distant water.
Smoking in the half-light, I sat watching my
companion, who was gazing seaward. It was one
of those times when silence is the best of all talk,
which only the utter fool kills with words. Pre-
sently I heard murmurs in the distance, and my
name sounded familiarly in my ears, with uncon i
plimentary adjectives attached. It was the first
time I had not turned up for the usual game, and
the regular party were wroth.
Mrs. Sabine looked round.
44 Your friends,” she said, 44 are calling you.’
44 To myself I said, 44 My friends be d d,”
but aloud—
-44 Thanks, but I know when I am well off.”
With a half smile— 44 Do you consider yourself
well off now ? ”
44 That,” I said, 44 remains to be seen.”
To the good all things are good, but some are
better than others, and I did not regret the cards.
We talked and talked in the shade of the deck-
house till the lights went out, when, handing my
companion her wraps, I went to make my peace
with the justly-indignant gamblers. It was the
only time I had disappointed them, and, except
the usual greetings, I had no further word with
Mrs. Sabine till one morning the ship swung
round in the Bay of Colombo.
* # #
When an ocean steamship stops at a port,
whatever other piece of imbecility you may be
guilty of, never go ashore with a party—that is,
a party of more than one. Insult the captain,
quarrel with the chief steward, or stay on deck
while they are coaling, if you like —anything is pre-
ferable. They are always of two kinds, these
parties. One set, the least objectionable, in-
vades the principal hotel, drinks unlimited
whisky, and thereafter proceeds to bargain
with and probably insult the native trader
with loud chaff and ungentle satire. The other,
and more objectionable set, hoists you forthwith
into a hired vehicle, and, with much jolting over
bad roads, rushes you from museum to church and
from church to museum, landing you back in the
evening a helpless wreck, with only the satisfac-
tion of knowing that nothing (in the guide-book)
has been left unseen, and that the whole is a con-
fused jumble in your mind. Therefore, on these
occasions, go always alone or with some congenial

spirit—a ship’s officer if you can.—even as in the
bush we were wont to go.
I knew all this as I stood on the deck watching
the red, white and green of Colombo in the
distance while perspiring passengers were pouring
over the side of the ship. Mrs. Sabine was stand-
ing near, as calm and imperturbable as ever.
4 4 Are you not going ashore ?” I asked.
44 Hardly, with this crowd,” she replied; 44 in
the afternoon for a while, perhaps.”
I made a suggestion.
44 The ship does not sail till to-morrow. There
is plenty of time for sight-seeing, should you care
about it, which I very much doubt. Let us have
lunch on board and drive to Mount Lavinia after-
wards. We can dine there and drive back in the
moonlight. Will you come ?”
She agreed.
You know, I daresay, that old drive through
the city and under the cocoa-nut palms to Mt.
Lavinia, and the stone-built hotel in the garden
on the hill, overlooking the sea. How many Aus-
tralians and returning Indians and globe-trotters
generally have made the pilgrimage, lunching
in the large hall and bargaining with the
native dealer in the broad verandah afterwards !
That afternoon the drive seemed pleasanter than
ever, although familiar enough. There had been
rain to lay the usual red dust and it was not too
hot. When we arrived we found that the mob of
sightseers had swept through the place like a
cyclone, and were off in another direction. The
gardens were deserted as we strolled through them;
and the wily Hindoo, who usually possessed the
front of the hotel, had packed up his wares and
was departing with a satisfied smile. Our friends
of the Hector had evidently not got the best of
that deal.
But they were gone, thank goodness ; gone with
their feeble jokes, noise, and idiotic attempt at
bargaining with men who could buy and sell them
twenty times an hour. We were left in peace.
When we returned from a stroll along the beach
our dinner was laid in the hall and we were alone.
It must have been a good dinner as Eastern din-
ners go, and the wine was more than fair, but
Mrs. Sabine made me forget both, for her eyes
had never seemed so bright—nor had she ever
talked like that before. I thought that at last I
had got to the real woman, but I had not—quite.
There are some days or nights of which the
memory remains always, and this was one.
Lounging on the broad verandah, the sea was
stretched before us, a sheet of silver in the bright
moonlight. A boat or two were in the distance,
with the thin smoke of steamers making their way
south round Point de Galle; on either side masses
of palms and ferns, so that we sat in the shade.
No sound was to be heard but the wash of the
waves on the shore and the rustling of the foliage
caused by the faint breeze that stole across from
the sea. For a longtime I sat silent, smoking and
thinking—thinking how all our pleasant times
must have an end—thinking of all the things a
man will think under the influence of a night like
that —and the pale face near me with the dark
eyes looked handsomer than ever in the faint
light.
Bah ! what was the good of thinking! The
cigar was finished and the evening done, the Cin-
galese driver was probably getting drunk in the
village, and it was time we started. As I moved
from the lounge my hand rested upon Mrs.
Sabine’s. Neither of us moved for a few moments
—then I spoke:
44 A perfect day and a perfect night—finished.”
No answer.
44 It is hard that we should have to go back
now.”
44 Why need we? ”
It was a whisper so faint that I hardly heard it,
but as I bent my head forward to catch the words
1 heard again almost inaudibly—
44 Why need we ? ”
* • *
I had been on board an hour or so next
morning when Mrs. Sabine joined the ship. She
had been staying with some friends in Colombo.
From that day till I left the ship at Naples I do
not think that we exchanged more than a dozen
words. That is all.
* * *
That is not all. Only this morning I came
across the following notice in the paper: — 44 At
Church, Margaret, widow of the late Chas.
Sabine, of Singapore, to , of . No
cards.” That, perhaps, is all.
GEORGE DANT.
THE AMATEUR RIDER.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Him going to ride for us ! Him —with the pants
and the eyeglass and all.
Amateur ! don’t he just look it —it’s twenty to one
on a fall.
Boss must be gone off his head to be sending our
steeplechase crack
Out over fences like these with an object like that
on his back.
Ride ! Don’t tell me he can ride ! With his pants
just as loose as balloons
How can he sit on his horse ! and his spurs like a
pair of harpoons;
Ought to be under the Dog Act, he ought, and be
kept off the course.
Fall! Why, he’d fall off a cart, let alone off a
steeplechase-horse.
Yessir ! the ’orse is all ready—l wish you’d have
rode him before;
Nothing like knowing your horse, sir, and this
chap’s a terror to bore;
Battleaxe always could pull, and he rushes his
fences like fun —
Stands off his jump twenty feet, and then springs
like a shot from a gun.
Oh, he can jump ’em all right, sir, you make no
mistake, ’e’s a toff;
Clouts ’em in earnest too, sometimes, you mind
that he don’t clout you off—
Don’t seem to mind how he hits ’em, his shins is
as hard as a nail,
Sometimes you’ll see the fence shake and the
splinters fly up from the rail.
All you can do is to hold him and just let him
jump as he likes,
Give him his head at his fences, and hang on like
death if he strikes ;
Don’t let him run himself out —you can lie third
or fourth in the race—
Until you clear the stone wall, and from that you
can put on the pace.
Fell at that wall once, he did, and it gave him a
regular spread,
Ever since that time he flies it —he’ll stop if you
pull at his head,
Just let him race—you can trust him —he’ll take
first-class care he don’t fall,
A.nd I think that’s the lot —but, remember, he
must have his head at the wall.
Well, he’s down safe as far as the start, and he
seems to sit on pretty neat,
Only his baggified breeches would ruinate any-
one’s seat —
They’re away—here they come—the first fence,
and he’s head over heels for a crown !
Good for the newchum, he’s over, and two of the
others are down!

THE BANJO.
HE’D COME BACK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The yarn was all lies, I suppose ; but it
wasn’t bad. A city bushman told it, of course,
and he told it in the trav’lers’ hut.
44 As true’s God hears me I never meant to
desert her in cold blood,” he said. 44 We’d only
been married about two years, and we’d got
along grand together; but times was hard, and
I had to jump at the first chance of a job, and
leave her with her people, an’ go up-country.”
He paused and fumbled with his pipe until all
ears were brought to bear on him.
44 She was a beauty, and no mistake; she was
far too good for me— I often wondered how she
came to have a chap like me.”
He paused again, and the others thought over it
—and wondered, too, perhaps. 44 The joker ”
opened his lips to speak, but altered his mind
about it.
44 Well, I travelled up into Queensland, and
worked back into Victoria ’n South Australia, an’
I wrote home pretty reg’lar and sent what money
I could. Last I got down onto the South Western
Coast of South Australia—an’ there I got mixed
up with another woman—you know what that
means, boys ?”
Sympathetic silence.
44 Well, this went on for two years, and then
the other woman drove me to drink. You know
what a woman can do when the devil’s in her ?”
Sound between a sigh and a groan, from “Lally”
Thompson— 44 My oath,” he said, sadly.
44 You should have made it three years, Jack ;”
interposed the joker, 4 4 you said two years before.”
But he was suppressed.
44 Well, I got free of them both, at last —drink
and the woman, I mean ; but it took another—it
took a couple of years to pull myself straight ”
Here the joker opened his mouth again, but was
warmly requested to shut it.
44 Then, chaps, I got thinking. My con*
science began to hurt me, and—and hurt worse
every day—it nearly drove me to drink again.
Ah, boys, a man —if he is a man—can’t expect to
wrong a woman and escape scot-free in the end.
(Sigh from 44 Lally” Thompson.) It’s the one
thing that always comes home to a man, sooner or
later —you know what that means, boys.”
Lally Thompson : 44 My oath ! ”
The Joker: 44 Dry up yer crimson oath ! What
d' you know about women ? ”
Cries of 44 Order ! ”
44 Well,” continued the story-teller, 44 1 got
thinking. I heard that my wife had broken her
heart when I left her, and that made matters
worse. I began to feel very bad about it.
I felt mean. I felt disgusted with myself.
I pictured my poor ? ill-treated, little wife
and children in misery and poverty, and
my conscience wouldn’t let me rest night, or
day (Lally Thompsom seemed greatly moved).
so at last I
made up my
mind to be a
man, and
make —what’s
the word ?”
“Repara-
tion,” suggest-
ed the joker.
44 Yes, so I
slaved like a
nigger for a
year or so, got
a few pounds
together, and
went to find
my wife. I
found out that
she was living
in a cottage
i n Burwood,
Sydney, and
struggling
through the
winter on
what she’d
saved from the
money her
father left her.
44 1 got a
shave and
dressed up
quiet and de-
cent. I was
older - looking
and more sub-
dued like, and I’d got pretty grey in those few years
that I’d been making a fool of myself—and some-
how I felt rather glad about it —because I
reckoned she’d notice it first thing—she was
always quick at noticing things—and forgive me
all the quicker. Well, I waylaid the school kids
that evening, and found out mine—a little boy
and a girl—and fine kids they were. The girl took
after her mother, and the youngster was the dead-
spit o’ me. I gave ’em half-a-crown each and told
them to tell their mother that some one would
come when the sun went down.”
Bogan Bill nodded emphatically.
“So at sundown I went and knocked at the
door ; it opened, and there stood my little wife
looking prettier than ever—only careworn.
* * *
Long, impressive pause.
“Well, Jack, what did she do? ” asked Bogan.
“ She didn’t do nothing.”
* * *
44 Well, Jack, what did she say? ”
Jack sighed and straightened himself up: “ She
said—she said : 4 Well, so you’ve come back.’ ”
Painful silence.
44 Well, Jack, and what did you say ? ”
44 1 said 4 yes.’ ”
44 Well—and so you had ! ” said Tom Moonlight.
44 It wasn’t that, Tom,” said Jack sadly, and
wearily— “lt was the way she said it I ”
* * *
Lally Thompson rubbed his eyes : “ And
what did you do, Jack ? ” he asked gently,
44 1 stayed for a year, and then I deserted her
again—but I meant it that time.”
44 Ah, well ! It’s time to turn in.”
JOE SWALLOW.


S INCE NELLIE
CAME
TO LIVE
ALONG THE CREEK
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
My hut is built of stringy-bark, the window’s
calico,
The furniture, a gin-case, one bush-table and
a bunk ;
Thick as wheat on my selection does the tower-
ing timber grow,
And the stately blue-gums’ taproots to the
bedrock all are sunk ;
Then the ferns spring up like nettles,
And the ti-tree comes and settles
On my clearing if I spell-o for a week ;
But I work for love of labour
Since I’ve got a handy neighbour,
And Miss Nellie’s come to live along the creek.
Time was when Death sat by me, and he stalked
me through the trees ;
Then my arm was weak as water, and my
heart a weary thing ;
I was sullen as a wombat on such still, wan
days as *hese,
And my wedges all were rusty, and my axe
had lost its ring.
Then a fear like sickness bound me,
And I cursed the trees around me,
For quite hopeless seemed the struggle I’d
begun ;
And at night-time, cowed and sinking,
I would sit there thinking, thinking,
Gazing grimly down the barrels of my gun.
Then I felt the bush must crush me with its
dreadful brooding wings,
And its voices seemed to mock me till I
thought that I was mad
Like the mopoke, and the jackass, and the
other loony things,
For beside my old dog, Brumbie, not a living
mate I had.
Then each sapling was a giant,
And the stumps were all defiant,
And my friends were very few and far to seek;
But the bush is bright and splendid,
And my melancholy’s ended,
Since Miss Nellie came to live along the creek !
I would swear she was the sweetest if the world
was full of girls :
She’s as graceful as a sapling, and her waist
is neat and slim ;
She is dimpled o’er with smiling, and has wattle-
blossom curls,
And her eyes peep out like violets ’neath her
sun-hat’s jealous rim.
When I think I see her flitting
On the sun crowned hill, or sitting
'Neath the fern-fronds where the creek sleeps,
deep and cool,
Ho, my stroke is straight and steady,
And the white chips run and eddy,
And I laugh aloud at nothing, like a fool.
Now my axe rings like a sabre,
ami my heart exults
with pride
When the green gums
sweep the scrub
down, and they
thunder and J
rebound,
And then lie with
limbs all
shattered,
reaching
out on either side,
Like giants killed in battle,
with their faces to the
ground
Now the bush has many pleasures,
And a wondrous store of treasures,
And a thousand tales its eerie voices speak ;
But its strange night hushes, seeming
Sent to hire to mystic dreaming,
Have no terrors now—Miss Nellie’s on the creek.
1 am happy when the thunder humps and
bellows on the hill,
And the tall trees writhe and wrestle with
the furies of the gale,
Or when sunshine floods the clearing, and the
bushland is so still
That I hear the creek’s low waters tinkle,
tinkle on the shale.
In the thought that she is near me
There's a charm to lift and cheer me,
And a power that makes me mighty seems to
flow
From Miss Nellie’s distant coo-ey,
Or her twin lips red and dewy
When she comes by here, and shyly calls me
“Joe.”
She can work from dawn to nightfall, and look
handsome all the day ;
At her smile my garden flourished, and the
vines grew green and strong,
And the bush falls back before it, and it strikes
the scrub away,
For it lingers ever with me, and it stirs me
like a song.
Now I work on in all weathers,
And the logs are merest feathers,
Nor my heart nor yet my hand is ever weak,
And a higher thing my prize is
Than all else that life comprises—•
Pretty Nell, who’s come to live along the creek.
Melb.
EDWARD DYSON.
THEIR
LAST BATTLE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Geordie Spooner was a good Orangeman. Abou
the aims and objects of Orangeiam and its history
and traditions he bad very hazy ideas, but he was
satisfied that it was anti-Irish in the main, and as
such, was a proper organisation for an Englishman
and a good Protestant to belong to. Really,
Geordie was nob a good Protestant; he was a
hard-faced, hard-fighting, hard-drinking, profane
worldling, but he hated the Irish, “ the lower
class of Irish,” for no other reason than constitu-
tional bias. It is natural for a “Geordie,” a Tyne-
sider, to hate Irishmen and Oousinjacks ; they
have hated both ever since they sent them mis-
sionaries in “the olden times,” and the
(term of endearment) ate them. This missionary
tradition may not be historically correct, but it is
as carefully preserved amongst Northumberland
miners as if it were.
Anyhow, reason or no reason, Spooner hated the
Irish. He was the only man in the mines who
could afford to allow himself the luxury of making
public his hatred and contempt. Horan’s shift at
the Better Spec, turned out some large, fierce,
hairy Milesians who could thump sap out of a red
gum block, but Geordie had thumped
the sap out of them, and was there-
fore justified in preserving “ Boyne
Water ” as his favourite air, especially
as he had a numerous following. The
man who is ready to do the fighting
always has.
There was only one man in the
district willing to offer himself whom
Spooner could not boast of having
beaten in fair fight, but whereas
Spooner was always prepared to
“take a round or two” out of any
other gentleman in the township or
locality at call, Dan Morrisey was
reserved for Sundays (long shift off)
and holidays. He and Morrisey had
ceased to engage in impromptu
“mills” after their first dozen battles.
There was a time when a hot word in
the bar would bring them together
with a crash of glass and furniture,
and a quick thud, thud, thud, of
trip-hammer blows, followed by a
rush for the paddock as soon as the
rival supporters —good sportsmen all
—could separate the combatants.
But these encounters invariably meant
the loss of a shift for one or the other
of the men, and often a bill for break-
ages at the pub., or trouble with the
police; so the rival champions were
impelled to take each other seriously,
and to regard a fight between them as
a special indulgence and not the sport of
an hour.
Their feud was an old one ; they had been fight-
ing on and off for five years, and had never had a
decisive battle. Geordie was confident he could
beat the Fenian, and Dan was certain he could
“ knock it out of” the “cocktail,” providing they
had time ; but there was never daylight enough in
a single day for either to get through, and the
toughest rough-and-tumble left neither much the
worse. Georgie was a nuggetty man—heavy,
unwieldy, and humpy with corded muscles; Dan
was taller, more active, with a gorilla-like reach
and mammoth bones, having a “raw edge” on
which “ yez could shtrike sparks wid a hammer, be
gob!” Geordie could hold an anvil on his chest
while a smith pointed a pick on it. Dan could
twist up horse-shoes and pokers with his bare
hands, and lift extraordinary weights with his
jaws. They were broken-nosed, battle-scarred
veterans both ; hard to bruise and hard to bleed,
and apparently about as sensible to pain as an
average rhinoceros.
Their last battle was fought on the n ght of the
“ glorious an’ blissid twelfb.” Geordie, who was
on the 12 o’clock shift, went on the war-path im-
mediately after knocking off that morning. He
believed that it was the duty of every right-think-
ing Orangeman to get drunk on the 12th July,
and to cripple as many Catholics as possible before
night. Towards midday, excited by beer, and
incited by a number of compatriots, Spooner
started out in quest of his old foe, Dan Morrisey.
It was, he felt, an appropriate day to witness the
demolition of the Fenian. But Dan was not
“ fit; ” he had been out of work for a fortnight,
and had taken such excellent advantage of his
leisure that for three or four days past he had .
been seeing snakes. Perhaps his friends, knowing
this, kept him out of the way, or, it was thought,
intelligent constable Finn, whose foresight was
remarkable, might have locked up the Hibernian
champion for the preservation of the peace. Any-
how, Spooner did not meet the only foeman
worthy of his steel, and was compelled to cele-
brate the day with the usual amount of “treble X ”
and a few inglorious scrimmages.
Late that night, when Geordie should have been
going on to work he was staggering home “ with
a load.” He had had a few hours’ sleep at the
Miners’ Rest, and, though substantially drunk,
had sufficient sense to know where he was going.
The track, after he had left the main and only
street, led through sparse timber and scattered
clumps of saplings, between the moaning shaft
and the black and silent engine-house of the
deserted Brown’s Hope mine, passing directly
under the bob-beam, and thence down the ugly,
rooky face of the steep hill, across the creek, and
home. As he approached the Brown’s Hope,
Geordie recollected that, exactly 12 months ago to
the hour, he had been met on this spot by a few
anonymous friends, “lower class Irish,” and
honoured with the most complete and satisfying
“ licking ” he had ever had in his life. The recol-
lection braced him up a little, and he prepared
for the worst. Sure enough there was Dan
Morrisey standing right in the track under the
beam, evidently “laying” for him. He recognised
the long, bony figure in the gloom.
Mr. Spooner knew that he was not cleared for
action, but he would not have turned aside then
for Brian Boru and all his descendants. He
would pass Morrisey without remarks if his foe
was not hungry for a fight, as he realised that he
was not in a condition to do himself and the
Orange cause justice, but he would not concede
an inch of the track to save his skin.
Spooner thought that Morrisey invited the en-
counter by bumping up against him, but that is
not feasible : “ hit first and hit hard ” was Mor-
uu , . „ , ,
risey’s politics, and it was Spooner who rolled
against the Irishman, and struck the blow he had
been saving for the occasion. Jhe two men met
in an unscientific scuffle, and Geordie went down
hard.
Regaining his feet, Geordie Spooner removed
his coat with drunken gravity, and faced his
enemy, using language appropriate to the occa-
sion, and a good deal of it. Dan maintained his
old position, and answered never an oath.
Geordie led again, but fought wide. He knew he
was all over the place, and reasoned to himself
sagely that he must be getting a fearful doing,
although he never knew where Dan landed. It
was a blind,. wild, impotent round, and_ Spooner
dropped again, going over heavily on the lumps of
reef.
Geordie could not tell how long the fight
lasted: he thought they might have been at it
an hour. He remembered that Dan was unusually
active on his feet, and never went down, whereas
he was thrown every round, and got badly
knocked about among'the stones. The last round
was short and furious. Reeling back after a
tremendous endeavour to cross-buttock the big
bushman, Geordie struck the railing round the
bob-pit, the railing smashed like match-wood
under his weight, and the champion of the Orange
cause made his bed on the rocks in the bob, with
a damaged head and a fractured arm.
Next morning short-sighted brother Proctor,
hurrying to work in the dusk, collided with the
body of a man hanging from the bob-beam at the
Brown’s Hope. Dan Morrisey had suicided by
hanging himself with his waist-belt. The feet of
the stiffened corpse just touched the ground, and
the clothes were nearly all torn from the body, and
there were signs all about of a protracted struggle.
A crowd soon assembled, and Geordie Spooner was
discovered in the pit. The Crown was anxious to
hang Spooner, but the evidence satisfied the jury
that the Orangeman had fought a knock-out with
a dead man.

“The Boys . ”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
On a certain Northern diggings, in that part of
tropical Australia where ‘‘the summer lasts for
nine months, and then the hot weather sets in,” a
few years ago, “the boys” occupied themselves
chiefly in gambling and drinking.
I arrived there one Christmas Eve, with .£53,
two horses, and a good turn-out.
I left early on Christmas morning, with a swag
on my back—half a blanket and a mosquito net—
and empty pockets.
I had always heard it was “a hot shop.” It
was too hot for me. One night griddled me up.
About a mile from the township, when search-
ing for a match, my finger-tip came against a coin.
An unexpected, solitary shilling !
While I stood looking at it, musingly, a horse-
man rode up and stopped
“ Find it ? ”
“ Well, yes ;in one of my pockets. I thought
I was cleaned out.”
“ Come back and I’ll shake you for it.”
I declined the mile’s tramp back, re-shouldered
ray swag, and went on. Presently, I heard a
cooey. Turning round, I saw my equestrian
acquaintance tearing towards me. I waited. Tie
galloped up and swung himself off his saddle i*
the best bush style.
“ Here you are, old man. I’m glad to accom-
modate you. I accidentally found it in my saddle
pouch.”
“ It ” was a dice-box and bones.
We threw. He won, of course.
“The boys would never have forgiven me if
I’d let you get away with that bob. So long, old
man.”
E.A.D.
