A LAY OF THE HERBERTON HOT SPRINGS.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Sixteen ten !” Jim Garbett said.
“Twenty quid !” said the station boss ;
“ Why, man, your father’d stand on his head
If he heerd you offer that for the boss.
Him as was foaled from a Kelpie mare,
Him as was sired by a Tamerlane colt;
S’help me, Jim, if you don’t take care,
God’ll strike you dead with a thunderbolt !”
This was the gist
Of his talk, and resist
It who could—Jim Garbett felt that he couldn’t;
So, though at first he swore he wouldn’t,
He made it a bargain, and gave his fist.
Twenty pounds ! It’s a heap of money !
Wish I had twenty pounds—don’t you ?
Twenty pounds ! A chap would feel funny
To find next morning he owned an old screw,
With the glamour off,
A mark for the scoff
Of every carnation station toff,
While his twenty pounds looked dismally blue.
But this was not Jim Garbett’s case :
His next morning fled apace
In feeding and grooming and watching his prize
With all an owner’s pride in his eyes.
For it needed only a glance to see
The colt was as good as his pedigree,
And you know what that was as well as me.
Dainty head and well-set feet,
Sloping shoulder, pastern neat,
Deep round chest, with a powerful loin,
Shape where strength and beauty join.
What was his color? Blaek, you dolt!
Ah, he was a picture, Jim Garbett’s colt.
All that night, while Garbett slept,
Visions impossible over him crept—
Fabulous sweepstakes, a wonderful cup,
A black colt entered, with owner up.
“ Woh, there ; steady ! ” Falls the flag;
They’re off ! Now, Mulligan, prove your brag !
Once round —still the brown mare leads —
Ah ! she’s none of your station weeds.
Half-way round —the pace is fast—
Jove, it’s a cracker ! —can she last?
Ten lengths from home the race is hers ;
All is over, Mulligan swears;
But he comes through the crowd like a crossbow
bolt,
Heads her, beats her —Jim Garbett’s colt 1
When the first streak of dawn
Took the night into pawn,
Jim Garbett got up from his bed with a yawn;
For the blacks were about
Spearing cattle, and doubt
Of his own little mob’s peace had found Garbett
out.
The black colt was yarded—’twould do him no
harm,
A run down the creek —so, with bridle on arm,
He reached the paddock, and rubbed his eyes
In unmistakeable surprise,
Which changed to fear, as with a frown
He saw that both the rails were down,
While in the dust at a gum-tree’s root
Were the marks of toes that had never worn boot,
And empty yard, and morning sky,
And mocking breeze that whistled by,
And a fat old crow who hovered on high
With a world of malice in his eye,
All seemed to echo Jim’s gasp of “ Tracks!”
With a horrible chorus of “ Blacks, blacks,
blacks I”
Down in the creek the black colt lay,
Making a hole in the yellow clay ;
Three spear-wounds in his glazing side
Showed too well how the black colt died ;
Swamp-oaks murmured and sedges sang
To soothe the black colt’s last strong pang;
The swollen creek, in turbid flood,
Bore the stain of the black colt’s blood;
Everything lived above, around,
The black colt only made no sound,
Stark and still in death he lay,
Never to race till the Judgment Day.
Not for him in the pride of strength
To romp home winner by a length
From a field of heroes whose mettle tried
Was known all over the country-side ;
Not for him the exulting crowd,
Cheers and plaudits, triumph proud;
Death on the pale horse shot his bolt—
No chance then for Jim Garbett’s colt!
Women may weep
For their dead, and steep
With tears the shroud which hides the last sleep ;
But men ! —Jim’s face grew stem and set,
Never a drop his lashes wet,
Yet there was that in the glint of his eye
Which the hardiest would not care to defy—
A something rigid about his pose,
And the curious way his fingers close,
And the heave with which his broad chest rose,
Spelling “Danger 1” plain as print to his foes.
Quietly back to fetch a spade
Walked Jim Garbett, and quietly made
A quiet grave by the side of the creek
Where the circling crows in vain might seek.
Back again, and quietly took
His gun from the pegs in the chimney nook ;
Stopped a moment to quietly say :
“Don’t wait for me, mother, at dinner to-day.”
Caught a horse, quietly rode away,
And—whatever ruse
In retreat they may choose,
To stand (if they wore ’em) in those blacks’ shoes,
I wouldn’t take Broken Hill as pay 1
It would tax most cracks
To follow the tracks
Left by that mob of fugitive blacks;
But Jim’s slow speed never seemed to relax.
Over the rocks where the scent lay weak,
Down again in the bed of a creek,
Grossing the swamp where the wild ducks hide,
Winding along the parched h'Aside,
Up the gorge where the river flowed,
Steadily, steadily Garbett rode.
Yet day was gone,
And night came on,
And still the blacks undoubtedly non
Sunt inventa, so down
Jim lay, with a frown,
While his hobbled horse cropped the herbage
brown.
Broken his sleep,
And at dawn’s first peep
He had risen, his harvest of vengeance to reap.
Not a moment he lagged,
Though his horse was fagged,
And foot after foot most wearily dragged.
Two miles were past,
And now at last
Jim’s pulses surged and his heart beat fast,
As, mounting a hill,
He saw with a thrill
Thin wreaths of blue smoke the next gully fill.
’Twas the end of his tramp,
He had come to the camp,
And felt he was free to work his will.
Out of his saddle Jim Garbett leapt,
Down the slope on his belly crept,
Kept as close as he could to the ground,
Paused on the verge, but heard no sound,
Looked to his gun, and changed the caps
(That was no time to risk mishaps)—
Then with his yell the forest rings,
As he jumps right into the—Boiling Springs !
Two days later, a horse dead lame
And a weary man to the homestead came,
The missus had to look twice at him
Before she shrieked, “ O Lord, it’s Jim !”
’Twas Jim indeed, he had failed to score, ;
And the blacks got off scot-free once morel
But the fame of the springs spread far ; ’twas said
They would make old Lazarus rise from the dead.
For fever and ague and rheumatiz,
Asthma, palsy, paralysis,
Liver complaint and chronic catarrh,
A never-failing cure they are.
And every year a greater crowd
Of pilgrims halt and crippled and bowed,
With ever greater vigour sings
The praise of Herberton hot springs,
Though they little know the phenomenal bolt
Of the niggers who speared Jim Garbett’s colt.
A. G. STEPHENS.
A
CHEAP
TURN OUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jaoker poked open the door of Wilson’s slab hut,
and stood peering in at the selector. Leaning
against the jamb, his six feet of ungainly anatomy
doubled across the entrance in many absurd
angles.
“ Mawn,” he said,
“ Homin’. ”
“ See yer spuds is cornin’ on well. Wan ter buy
a gun ?”
Wilson took the gun offered him, weighed it in
his hand, tried the lock, and ran his eye along the
barrels.
“ Splendid duok-gun,”said Jacker. There were
lagoons just up the creek, and the reputation of
every gun in the district had to be made amongst
ducks.
Wilson was a selector holding about 180 acres,
thrifty, energetic, and progressive. Jacker was
also a selector, dumped on the adjoining 100 acres.
Jacker’s apparent mission in life was to officiate
as a lay-figure of some kind. He felled a tree
here and there in a desultory sort of way, and he
once put up about a chain of dog-leg fence, but
had spent most of his time since in repentance.
He owned a one-roomed hut, containing some
home-made furniture, a pannikin, and a tin plate;
he also owned a dog with three legs, and the gun.
“ Goin’t’ get married.”
“ Jee-rusalem 1 you can’t affort t’ marry, can
yeh ?”
“ Can if I sell th’ gun.”
“ But she aint worth more’n 30 bob !” ejaculated
Wilson.
“Well,” answered his visitor, miraculously
impassible, “they’ll marry yeh up town for half-a-
orown, I’ve heard.”
Wilson gasped. “Who’s to be the missus?” he
asked.
“ Dunno. Mean t’ advertise. I’m tired of
livin’ alone, that’s what’s the matter.”
“ Well, I’m blowed if you don’t bang Bannagher.
Here, take yer gun; I wouldn’t do yeh such a
dirty trick as t’ buy it under the circumstances 1”
Jacker took the gun, and tucked it up under his
arm. “ Maybe someone else will,” he replied,
sulkily, and scrambled off down the track.
Next day, Wilson heard that Aldon had bought
Jacket’s gun for 255., and in less than a week
Jaoker returned from town with a wife and
family. He had married an encumbered widow.
Melb.

A PROBLEM IN
PETTICOATS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was matron of an Australian metro-
politan hospital, and her name was Ann Drink-
water. In person she was liberal, not massive;
her curves round and generous. A wreath of hair,
fine, black, and growing rather low down on her
forehead, was tucked demurely beneath the snowy
cap impotent to veil its luxuriance. Her com-
plexion was pale and pure; her large brown eyes
shone with serene brightness ; her mouth was full
and firm, with red, ripe lips; hands white, shapely,
strong and tremorless. At first sight, perhaps,
she did not strike you as handsome, but her face
and figure, critically analysed, were not unsug-
gestive of amorous possibilities. Nevertheless,
men did not fall in love with her—not in the ordi-
nary sense, at least. Because from Ann Drink water
there emanated an atmosphere not precisely of
frigidity, but anti-sensual, almost anti-sexual. Not
her modest garb alone; not the nature of her
avocation only ; but every demulcent glance of her
luminous eyes, every chaste movement of her
becoming person, every inflection of her clear,
passionless voice, proclaimed that she had either
put away or had never known the fitful fever
called love. Here was a woman you at first glance
knew had no thought to spare upon frivolities,
but fully realised the grim earnestness of a world
in which were so many aches and pains, so
many fevers and dislocated limbs. Further
acquaintance revealed that only in the per-
formance of her duties did she find pleasure;
that for her true bliss lurked in boluses, blisters,
leeches, and cognate matters —not in lovers’ sighs
and caresses. Yet she was no mere machine-
made nurse and matron. She brought all the
poetry of her nature and the imagination of her sex
to the proper preparation of pills and poultices.
She introduced fancy and high art into the
administration-of ipecacuanha and calomel. The
Chief, all unused to gush, declared that the
matron’s methods of rolling bandages, presenting
paregoric, and supervising the final details of a
death-bed, were visions of supernal joy. And she
was kind and soothing to patients—to male
patients, that is. But there was innate master-
fulness about her very mildness. Her hand and
step, light as gossamer, were strong as steel. Her
touch, comforting in its coolness, was strangely
compelling. Her movements, devoid of fluster,
were swift and certain. She was a born nurse,
perfected by arduous practice.
* # *
She was no callow girl. In her neat, but
sombre, hospital uniform she looked quite 30.
Had she worn bright colors, had her exu-
berant tresses been permitted to wanton in
love-locks, curls and waves, untrammelled _by
cap, and had a robust male arm been occasion-
ally allowed to wan
der round her supple
waist, she would
doubtless have seem-
ed years younger.
But even to imagine
such licenses in such
connection ' was pro-
fanation from which
the most; audacious
male might shrink.
For, nunlike, this
woman appeared set
apart from the casual-
ties of a wantom
world and its
love-code. The medi-
cal men who knew her
extended to her that
admiration which
they would have felt
for a skilled male col-
league, coupled, how-
ever, with the ex-
aggerated esteem and
respect medical men
entertain for a woman
so absolutely healthy
that she has" never
subjected herself to
theif professional re-
view. The male
patients adored her
with an adoration not
untinctured by awe.
So they might have
regarded the Madon-
na in a T9th century
matron’s ia p and
cuffs;
'*.V *
The nurses, the
female patients and
visiting womenfolk,
did,'not love matron
Drinkwater. They
feared her. And this
the ipote because she
Was tflcoraprehensible
to them. Her woman-
ly annor revealed
none of those chinks
through which the
feline sex appraise
each other’s faults
and vices. The
most painfully vir-
tuous, woman must
condemn and may despise her erring sister. But
the successful exercise of her perspicaciousness and
the recognition'll! the sinner of the female frailty
dormant,, yet existent, in herself, prevents the
chastest creature from really hating the most in-
continent —always provided the peccancy of the
latter doss not infringe on the preserves of the
former. When, however, neither female saints
nor sinher§ can "detect even peccadilloes in another
woman, the singularity of the phenomenon terrifies
them. And that which women fear they also hate.
And Drinkwater her conscience as lightly
and openly as her piquant apron - --neither fear
nor shame peeped out from behind her sedateness.
The wives of the married doctors spoke of the
matron as a “highly superior person,” but, being
uncomfortable in presence of so much perfection,
declined to like her. They liked her the less
because she understood, without taking advantage
of her knowledge, their respective husbands better
than did the ladies themselves. Jealous of her
intellect, they spared her person. The nurses
secretly rebelled against the emotionless rigor of
her rule, and were openly dismayed by her unfail-
ing detection of their laches. They obeyed her in
trembling. As regards feminine visitors, the
matron looked them through and through, under-
standing them; so that weeping women without
sorrow who, in the enforced absence in hospital of
their men folk, sought the devious consolations of
an Australian city, knew they were read by the
woman who spoke with grave sympathy to their
husbands or sweethearts, and without it to them.
And, as for female patients, Ann Drinkwater did
her duty to them to the last jot and tittle of the
law. Yet, even when they were dying, they
fancied they could discern in her eyes a look which
tranquilly assured them they were de trap and that
the matron would prefer not to meet them in
heaven.
# * *
In a manner not precisely maternal or
sisterlike, or saintlike, but rather a mixture of all
three with some unknown quantity, the matron
was benign to all the unmarried medical men and
sucking sawbones connected with the hospital.
And there were many methods by which she could
and did ameliorate the lot of those who had no
duly licensed woman to care for them. To be
sick, sorry, desolate, afflicted or jilted was to
possess sure passport to her benevolence. This
was, doubtless, the reason why she displayed an
indulgence almost tender to Charlie Hooper, tire
senior assistant-surgeon—a fair-headed, curly-
headed, blue-eyed and feminine-looking young
butcher known to his intimates as “Charlotte.”
His unmistakable delicacy engaged her pity ; the
defective state of his linen and the absence of
letters in a feminine hand revealed to her that he
needed and lacked feminine ministration. She
adopted him at sight. Thenceforward he knew
no care or pain a mother, sister and saint com-
bined could relieve him of. While all the other
objects of her bounty repaid it with astonished
thankfulness and veneration, Charlie accepted it
with utmost insouciance, as if conferring, not
receiving, favours. He alone ever ventured the
slightest approach to familiarity or facetiousness
with her. “It jars upon me to hear _Charlotte
chaffing Sister Ann,” declared the chief “it’s
like joking over the sacraments in church.” But
Charlie didn’t care. He would have alternated
coughing and bad puns in the very portal of para-
dise. And Ann Drinkwater listened to his jocu-
larity with an air of seraphic tolerance; and left
his door and the door of her own_ room, which
adjoined, open of a night that she might hear him
when he coughed. And she would rise a dozen
times in the dark watches to give him
physic, smooth his pillow, and lave his
hot, curly head with eau-de-cologne. She
supervised his food and garments, made him
pretty, and kept him neat. Nor did the most
censorious tongue wag loosely concerning these
things, chiefly because the matron was above
suspicion of earthly motive, partly because Hooper
was—save at the operating-table, where both his
skill and nerve were robustly masculine —essen-
tially feminine. He loved music, flowers, birds,
perfumes, and all pretty and dainty things, but
seemed to avoid the society of girls, while indulg-
ing in no masculine pursuits. His voice, tricks of
manner and appearance were all girlish. His
relations with the matron seemed those of a
thoughtless, merry girl with an elder graver
companion of the same sex.

Ann ?” remarked Charlie, one morning, early.

He—the man, not the lion—was brought in about
10.30, in a torn and well-blooded dress-suit, his
ugly, great fingers plastered with diamond rings,
wearing a gold chain like a ship s cable and a
locket big as a soup-plate, with a diamond like a

JENKS: “ Do you know, Miss Smith is so thunderingly polite that she
calls a laughing-jackass a smiling-johnnie donkey.
poisoning, he’ll recover. The fellow has the most
disconcerting eyes I ever saw belonging to any-

Here a nurse tapped at the door. “If you
please, m’m, will you come to the new casualty;
he’s going on dreadfully.”
The matron followed quietly to look at the new
patient.
1 * * *
He wasn’t pretty to look at. He was very
tall and very wide. His huge carcase ridiculously
dwarfed the hospital bed. He was black and
hairy, making the sheets a painful white by con-
trast. His mauled chest was swathed in ban-
dages, through which the blood, brown and caked,
unhandsomely showed. Face, head, body, and
limbs were covered with coarse, black hair; his
complexion, pallid through blood - loss, was a
lively gamboge; great knotted cords stood
out from his neck ; his arms and legs—he
had spumed his coverings—were enormously
muscular. His eyes were wonderful and hor-
rible, appearing brown, green, black, or red by
turns. But, whichever tint his eyes took —
whether the pupils, as they one moment appeared,
were big and round as shillings, or mere narrow
slits, as they looked the next—they burned with
a lustre so intolerable that you could feel their
power with your back turned to their owner. _ The
indescribable odor which emanates from carnivora
seemed to surround him and was not mitigated by
the evil perfume of a cigar which he smoked
despite the expostulations of a nurse.
As the matron entered the ward he, moved
partly by pain, chiefly by innate savagery, kicked
out the foot of his bed, the iron sheet crumpling
as if smitten by a blacksmith’s sledge.
“Who the devil may you be?” he affably en-
quired of the matron as she walked without hurry
to his bedside.
“lam the matron, and I allow neither smoking
nor bad language here !” she replied \yith her
usual deliberate tranquillity. She advanced,
quietly and deftly removed the cigar from between
the fellow’s great yellow fangs, and, walking to
the window, threw the weed out.
For one moment he was staggered by the
audacity of the thing. The next he roared a
string of curses as vile as ever passed human lips,
and made as if to spring at the matron. The
other nurses, fearing actual violence, stampeded.
The matron placed herself at the foot of the bed
and gazed steadfastly into the brute’s eyes.
When he perceived the attitude of the matron
he sank back on his pillow, with a hyaena-like
laugh, and fixed his baleful glare upon her, as if
with the power and will to blight and scorch her
dead where she stood.
“ I’ve been used to your sort—she-tigers,” he
snarled.
At this moment the Chief, accompanied by
Hooper and the dispenser, entered and walked
down the ward. The Chief, about to speak,
checked himself and watched.
For the modest, Madonna-like, Ann Driukwaier
and Signor Roberto ( Anglice , Roberts), the brutal
beast-tamer, were engaged in a singular duel-a
duel of eyes, will, mesmeric power, call it what we
may to hide our ignorance.
And so they continued to gaze at one another —
she, the gentle and pure-looking woman ; he, the
man-beast. The scene was redeemed from mere
theatrical disp’ay by its intensity.
Minutes passed. None spoke or moved. Then,
swiftly, without warning, a change came over the
face of the man. A shade flitted across it. The
malignant gleam in his eyes slowly faded. Then
his form became limp; he tried unavailingly to
rise; fell back ; glanced, with a new spasm of
ferocity, at the matron ; shut his eyes, partially
opened them, and, as face and form settled into
rigidity, fell back, apparently sound asleep.
“He’ll be quiet now for a time, sir,” she said,
turning to the Chief.
The medical men took advantage of the lull to
examine and dress the beast-tamer’s wounds.
When lie awoke, though still a beast, he was a
comparatively quiet one. Ho blasphemed in a
minor key. The matron visited him at night. He
pretended not to recognise her, but he did not
abuse her, which, possibly, was the nearest
approach to civility of which he was capable.
# * *
Charlie’s cough became worse. Charlie’s

once. Was there anywhere he could go for some
weeks, perhaps months? Charlie said he knew
some people, squatters, way far-back. He coma

for the departure of

came when Charlie’s

and Charlie, looking

and say how he was ;
and, in the next breath,

* # *
The months passed. The torridness of
summer had burnt down to the heats of autumn,
and they had declined to the premonitory chills of
winter. Charlie had written often. He was
much better. In the dry interior, far from the
city’s sweats and stenches and everlasting fuss and
fury, living much in the open air, he had
taken a new and stronger grip on life. He would
return soon, but not to stay. In the hospital
things were as usual. People were carried in
bruised, battered, broken, burnt with fever or
fire. Some were carried out dead; some 'Walked
out —others remained. Among these latter was
Signor Roberto, now almost well. Coeval with
his physical there had been a subtle, but per-
ceptible, mental improvement in the animal. At
first he had broken out again and again, though
with diminished fury on each occasion. They
always sent for the matron, whose mere presence
calmed him. As time went on he displayed a pre-
dilection for that presence. In the fever bom of
his wounds he asked for her, and when she came
she seemed to have the power to charm away his
pain. He was still a brute, perhaps, nevertheless
a humanised one.
* * *
Charlie had not written for some weeks,
when the hospital staff received a surprise from
him in the shape of wedding-cards. He had mar-
ried the squatter’s daughter in the far-back ; also,
he had inherited some money. He would wield
lance and saw no more.
Shortly after the delivery of the mail, young
Wakeham, Charlie’s locum tenens, wanting the
matron on business, went to her sitting-room.
Knocking, and thinking he was bidden enter,
he went in. A long ray of cold sunlight stretched
across the room. Touched by its light lay
Ann Drinkwater in a heap on the floor. In
one hand she clasped Charlie’s wedding-cards.
Her splendid hair, escaped from control, streamed
around her. Her face was pale and cold as mar-
ble. She had fainted. Wakeham would have
called assistance, but he was a discreet lad, and
observed that whichmade him pause. The room was
literally strewn with wedding finery—female gar-
ments which looked as if they had been subjected
to a cyclone. There was a marvellous white-silk
dress, torn, trampled upon and dirty ; a veil rent
across ; gloves wrenched in twain; a wreath pulled
to pieces.
# # *
The hospital authorities had another sur-
prise. Matron Drinkwater respectfully tendered
her resignation. She and the beast-tamer, strange
to say, left the institution the same day. One
month afterwards they were married. “God help
her !” groaned the chief ; “ the best nurse I ever
knew. That savage will trample her life out
before three months are over !”
* # •
Two years afterwards Houpla’s circus and
menagerie were again showing in that Australian
metropolis. The great HoupLi was lunching with
a newspaper man.
“I was up at A recently,” said the latter.
“Who d’ye think I met?”
“ The Lord knows; perhaps an honest news-
paper man. Nothing is impossible.”
“No ; I met that beastly lion-tamer of yours—
Roberto, the fellow that killed old Feefum.”
“In gaol—l presume?”
“Not much. His hair was cut decently, which
yours is not, Houpla, and he was well and quietly
dressed, and carried no stage diamonds. Also he
was walking with a lady and wheeling a pram., in
which were two cubs, apparently twins. I dined
with him and his wife. He was the meekest mail
and the lady the most calmly autocratic woman 1
have met for long years. She was a hospital
matron —maiden name, Ann Drinkwater.”
“Great Caesar! what a lion-tamer she would
have made !” said lloupla, excitedly.
Maorilaud.

THE RIDING
OF
THE REBEL
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
He was the Red Creek overseer, a trusted man and
true.
Whose shoulder never left the wheel when there
was work to do ;
Through all the day he rode the run, and when the
lights grew dim
The sweetest wife who ever loved would wait and
watch for him.
She brought him dower of golden hair and eyes of
laughing blue,
Stout heart and cunning bridle-hand to guide the
mulga through,
And when the mob was mustered from the box
flats far and wide
She loved to mount the wildest colts that no one
else would ride.
And once it chanced a wayward steed, half-
mouthed and roughly broke,
Denied the touch of gentle hand and gentler
words she spoke,
And, plunging forward like the ship that feels the
autumn gales.
He reared and lost his footing and fell backwards
on the rails.
Her husband bent above her with cold terror at
his heart
The form was still he loved so well, the wan lips
would not part,
And all the day in trance she lay, but when the
stars smiled down
He heard his name low-whispered and he claimed
her still his own.
And afterwards he spoke his fear: “ Heart’s Love,
if you should die I
Unless you take your orders from some other man
than I,
You shall never finger bridle, never mount on
horse’s back,
Till the outlaw on Glenidol is a broken lady’s
hack!” J
There s an outlaw on Glenidol that is known
through all the West,
And three men’s lives are on his head, bold riders
of the best;
The station lads had heard the sneer that travelled
far and wide
And flung the answering challenge: “Come and
teach us how to ride ;
Roll up, ye merry riders all, whose honor is to
guard,
We’ve mustered up the ranges and The Rebel’s in
the yard—
His open mouth and stamping foot and keen eye
flashing fire
Repeat the temper of his dam, the mettle of his
sire.
Roll up, ye merry riders all, from hut and camp
and town:
You’ll have to stick like plaster when the stock-
yard rails go down,
But the boss will come down handsome, as the
boss is wont to come.
To the first who brings The Rebel under spurs and
greenhide home.
And the stockmen heard the challenge from the
Cooper to the Bree,
And rode from hut and cattle-camp by one and
two and three
To keep their horseman’s honor clean and play a
hero’s part
lo best the bold Glenidol boys and break The
Rebel’s heart.
And Ruddy Neil, the breaker, from the Riverine
came through
With all the latest breaking-gear and all the wiles
he knew,
But ere the saddle was secured, before a girth was
drawn,
The Rebel’s forefoot split his skull—they buried
him at dawn.
Marora Mick, the half-caste, from the Flinders
River came
To give the South-the-Border boys a lesson at the
game,
But he got a roguish welcome when he entered
New South Wales
For The Rebel used his blood and brains to paint
the stockyard rails.
And Mulga Jack came over from the Yuinburra
side—
The horse was never foaled, they say, that Mulga
could not ride ;
With a mouth as hard as a miser’s heart, a will
like the Devil’s own,
The Rebel made for the Stony Range with the
man who wouldn’t be thrown.
The Rebel made for the Stony Range, where the
plain and the scrub land meet,
And the dead boughs cracked at his shoulder-
blade, the stones leapt under his feet,
And the ragged stems of the gidyeas cut and tore
as they blundered past,
And Jack lay cold in the sunset gold—he had met
with his match at last.
And once again the challenge rang, the bitterer
for scorn,
And spoke the bold Glenidol boys, their jackets
mulga-tom:
“ A week have we been hunting him and riding
fast and hard
To give you all another chance—The Rebel’s in
the yard.”
And the stockmen heard the challenge from the
Cooper to the Bree.
But “I’m getting old,” “I’m getting stiff,” or
“ I’ve a wife, you see,”
Came whispered to the border, and the horse they
could not tame
Had saved Glenidol from disgrace and cleansed a
sullied name.
But ere the reddening sun went down and night
on the ranges broke
A stranger youth to the slip-rails rode, and
fastened his horse and spoke
Straight to the point, “ This colt of which the
whole of the West has heard,
I’ll ride him;” —and active and lithe of limb he
looked as good as his word.
He bridled The Rebel in failing light, and saddled
the colt and drew
Girth and crupper and breastplate tight and mea-
sured his length anew.
He mounted The Rebel and gave the word, and
a j clat ? rm 3 rads down > .
6 Jp U j aw 4ea pt the open gate and into
r l6 j W f brown ;
But he settled himself to the soothing voice and
a i tbe fondling hand,
As it followed the curve of his arching neck from
Tr . wither to forehead-band;
His flanks were wet with the fresh-sprung sweat,
a A 3 sl ?°ulders lathered with foam,
And he bent to the bridle and played with the
a jT® as he came at a canter home.
And the boys were dumb with wonder, and sat,
wt ai e x ® tted Greek overseer
Was first to drop from the stockyard fence and
give him a hearty cheer ;
He raised hi S hat in answer and—the golden hair
a j u ' ree ’ . . ,
And the blue eyes lit with laughter as she shouted
~ merrily .
You can reach me down my bridle, give my
girths and saddle back,
fior the outiaw of Glenidol is a broken lady’s
GLENROWAN.
Her Portrait.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Samuel was working on a survey-party on the
East Coast of Maoriland. That day the boss had
sent him down to the settlement for some needed
stores and the weekly mail.
Towards evening he was returning to camp.
He had a heavy load, but he had also that in the
pocket of his jumper which made his seventy
pounds of miscellaneous cargo seem like a bundle
of feathers. “She” had written to him, enclosing
her photo., and often had Samuel paused on his
way to refresh himself with a glance at it.
Straight up the steep ridge went the cut-line he
was following, which led to the big station on the
highest peak of the range. Here the bush was
filled with the faint blue smoke which came from
a huge rata that had been burning for the last
three days.
Just where the line widened out into the station
avenue Samuel paused again, up-ended his swag
against a convenient stump, and fumbled in his
pocket for the precious picture.
It was not a very good one. Neither, perhaps,
was the original ; but, in Samuel’s bush-bred eyes,
no cigarette-placard could have been more divinely
beautiful.
Along, creaking, groaning crash, a volcano of
upward-pouring sparks, and a roaring, thundering
avalanche of timber came fair down the line. The
ground trembled with the shock, and the standing
bush bent with the blast it made. The topmost
crown of glossy leaves and scarlet blossoms reached
within a bare ten feet from Samuel and his swag.
Samuel was blown like a thistle-seed into a con-
venient clump of supplejacks, the photo., not
much crumpled, still safe in his hand.
“ If it hadn’t been for her picture that blessed
tree would have caught me,” said he.
Subsequently, for reasons connected with the
picture, he regretted that the tree had not caught
him. But that is not part of this chapter.
WAIHOA.
Few people can form anything approaching
a just idea of the wealth of picturesque scenery to
be found in New South Wales, which in older
countries would be a ceaseless source of attraction
to myriads of tourists from all parts of the civilised
world ; or the extent to which it has been made
accessible by the State-owned railways to the
great mass of holiday-makers. In this respect the
residents of Sydney are, perhaps, more favored
than are the inhabitants of any other city. The
railways have brought to their very threshold, as
it were, the manifold charms of lake and river.
mountain and sea-side, valley and forest, enabling
them to exchange the reeking, disease-germ-laden
a ir of crowded streets and lanes for the pure and
exhilarating atmosphere of the country. North,
west, and south, the hands of Nature have
provided scenic marvels in abundance for the
enjoyment of Man. The noble Hawkesbury
and the broad Nepean, with the ever-chang-
ing landscape on either side ; Brisbane Water,
Lake Macquarie, Lake George, and other in-
land miniature seas, abounding with fish and
game ; the fairy-like Illawarra district, rich in
ferns and palms; the ever-popular National Park,
epitomising all that is lovely in Australian
Nature; the Fitzroy, Mery la, Wentworth,
Katoomba, and other Falls ; Govett’s Leap ; the
Kanimbla, Grose, and other valleys; Jenolan,
Wombeyan, and other caves, form only a portion
of the many wonders of the great holiday land
placed at the national disposal. Further afield
are the Western> Northern , and Southern table-
lands, immense regions, considerable portions of
which remain as virgin soil to the foot of the
tourist, as does also the greater part of the country
beyond Nowra and Jervis Bay. If the latter-day
Australian fails in imbibing a taste for art or
poetry it certainly will not be from' lack of the
elements of inspiration. Directly he has left the
city an d its octopus suburbs behind him he
becomes surrounded by the beautiful in Nature,
an 4 instinctively realises the force of Ebenezer
Elliott’s remark that “ the further from the haunts
of men, the nearer is he to Nature’s God.”
ATHLETICS AND SMOKING.
Ought athletes to smoke ? Yes and no. Opinions
are considerably at variance on the subject, but
the verdict of most modern scientists would lead
to the conclusion that youth, while training, should
abstain from smoking pipes, cigarettes, or strong
cigars. A mild cigar, on the other hand, may
safely be indulged in, and the latest novelty,
Frossard’s Oavour cigars, can be recommended to
one and all—they are mild, aromatic, and in every
respect suitable for the successful sportsman, as
well as for the one who is out of it. A packet of
eight only costs one shilling. Athletes, go for
them and be victorious !

XMAS !
XMAS !
“Jehosophat."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am a bucking rider, as the boys on Bando know,
I care for nothing here on earth and little down
below,
And Watson, of Willora, on the old Port Darwin
track,
He offered me a camel if I’d use it for a hack.
“ Jehosophat ” he called it, and it had a meaning
smile,
Extending round its features —well, it may be,
half-a-mile;
And it knelt to let me mount in a way so sweet to
see,
That I rammed the spurs in boldly and requested
it to “gee.”
It geed; at least, like thunder all its hind part
rose up straight,
And I went whizzing to its neck, most painful to
relate.
It geed; its front part reared aloft —but here mere
words must fail,
For I was clinging to its hump, I think astride its
tail.
And it started a performance which it seemed to
think a trot,
But I wish to state impressively I certainly did
not.
’Twas a painful combination of some thirty Irish
jigs,
And its little tail went whirling round precisely
like a pig’s.
Whilst we gallivanted onward like an epileptic fit,
I was searching all that camel for a kindly place
to sit.
There were two great slopes around me to its neck
and to its tail,
And a big ridge in the middle rather sharper than
a nail.
I had backed the wildest outlaws from Willora to
Taring,
But the riding of an earthquake is a very different
thing ;
I gripped the hump with both my arms and yelled
“Jehosophat!”
And the camel gave a grunt of joy and put on
steam at that.
And when we settled to our stride, I had no
strength to swear,
And when I found the breath to curse the camel
wasn’t there.
But the boys, they told me firmly, when they came
to pick me up :
“Bill Grady, you have got the nag to win the
Melbourne Cup !”
Jehosophat, he lives and smiles, but I m a shat-
tered wreck.
I broke three ribs against his hump, one shin upon
his neck;
My bones have all been somehow set, and Watson’s
eyes are black,
But I’ll never, never use again a camel for a hack !
Brisbane.
G. ESSEX EVANS.

The Ferryman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mitchell stood six feet in his stockings. By the
water’s edge, the boat; behind the shingle bed,
the green turf; on the turf, the cottage. Part
dairyman, part stock-raiser, and a big lump digger
—that is the ferryman.
“Familiarity breeds contempt.” You see the
mariner of 30 years lash the rope of the sail in its
cleat. You see the engine-driver of ten years
fall in a moment under the ponderous wheels of
the locomotive. You watch the successful pilot of
a hundred races thrown, dragged by the stirrup,
and brained. Why should the ferryman be an
exception? .
Was it a river? Yes, when it rained; in fine
weather it was only a creek. But then it often
rained. At such times the water changed its
colour and became a sour white. There is a
caution in that which is fickle.
Like a million other streams, its destiny was the
Pacific. Under cover of the clouds the sea found
time to whisper as they embraced :
“ I am thy mother—some children played upon
the shore to-day —I coveted them, but the tide was
spent. Bring me a corpse of man to make, me
sport.”
And the river promised.
That night the sky was filled with silver cords.
It was the rain. The river gurgled and rose.
Mitchell took the lantern at 10 o’clock, and went
to see to his boat. He found water all round it.
The river eyed him cunningly. The boat never
warned him. Below the boat was a recent eddy—
breathless, triumphant. It had but that minute
torn a huge hole in the shingle.
Putting the lantern on the bank, Mitchell
stepped into the water to drag the boat further up
the beach. He clutched the gunwale, and felt
that his feet were drawn right under. At the
same instant the boat began to pay out broadside
down the stream.
Mitchell opened his mouth to cry for help.
That attempted appeal was his death-warrant,
swimmer though he was. In an instant his head
went under.
The water poured down his throat. He felt his
mother, in fancy, gently stroking his cheek. He
loosed his grasp of the boat. When he rose, the
power to struggle was for ever gone.
Mitchell’s wife took the tin lamp, and, with her
daughter, went in quest of her husband. The
other children crowded in the doorway. The
lantern still burned, but it rested on the stones.
Where were the hands that had brought it there ?
“ Coo-ee !”
No answer.
“ Coo-ee-ee !”
Silence is a mocker.
The river was too high to cross to the town-
ship, but one of the boys rode five miles down
the bank to a neighbour’s place for counsel and
assistance. There is sublimity in the compulsory
craving for sympathy at such a time.
* * *
Ex-lieutenant Hamborough, once a soldier, now
a beachcomber, rubbed his huge hands with satis-
faction. The sea had been rough. There was a
fine flat of sand with a bank of six feet at high-
tide mark. All night had the sea sluiced those
tons of stuff.
He reckoned 20 ounces at least.
He took his box with its plush and blanket
ripples and sallied out. Gold sticks in hair. May-
be that is how woman first learned to feed vanity
from effect.
Descending the bank, he began to shovel the
useful sand into heaps.
He stopped, for the shovel struck a portion of
flesh. He bored more. It was a man’s back. He
uncovered the lot, and identified it easily. It was
his old friend, Mitchell, who had fought by his
side at Cawnpore. And the knots of 20 years
worn into the rope with which he had carried his
box were easier to unravel than the wherefore of
this strange meeting arranged by fate.
Do not play at hazards with danger; keep well
on the upper side.
Maoriland.
F. DE BAKKER.
Voices.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There are three mighty Voices that alway
Cry out to God to speed His Judgment Day.
The Voice of Devils, weary long ago
Of dragging souls to Everlasting Woe.
The Voice of Saints who hear, while anthems
swell
In Heav’n, the wail of sinners doomed to Hell.
The Voice of Man —sick of his desperate
Long throwing ’gainst the leaded dice of Fate.
All things are weary of the Strife and Stress —
In God alone is there no weariness?
V.J.D.
A sulphur-spring has been found near
Frankston (Vic.). When they strike a treacle-
mine close by, Frankston will become a great
juvenile sanatorium.
The Maoris at the Barrier (M.L.) carefully
stripped of any rings or other valuables every
corpse which came into their hands from the
Wairarapa wreck—which is just what most civi-
lised white men would have done. Where the
difference comes in is that, so far as can be dis-
covered, every separate article, down to the very
smallest, was religiously handed over to the police.
Thus the cable :
Nov. 24th.—The Chinese
fought gallantly, and re.
fused to accept quarter.
The Japanese lost heavily.
The Chinese army occupy-
ing Port Arthur, estimated
to number between 15,000
and 20,000 men, was vir-
tually annihilated.
Nov. 26th. —In the attack
on Port Arthur, the Ja-
panese lost 250, and the
Chinese between 1000 and
2000 men. . . Many of the
guns were loaded, the
Chinese having fled with-
out firing them. Th"
Chinese officers led the
flight.
Which yarn is true? Probably neither.
A Buxletin correspondent :
In the newspaper accounts of the Wairarapa disaster all
the large slices of advt. are plastered on to the saloon
passengers. If a pressman wants “ copy ”on any event of
this nature he always goes to the first class traveller, who
often happens to be the biggest gas-bag on board, and
gives a tangled description of saloony bravery, particu
larly his own. If the crew behave with noticeable zea
the company gets the bulk of commendation for rakinv
together such a reliable lot of men. But the steerage
voyao-er very seldom “ comes in ” at all. A week’s sea-
sickness at 30s. the trip is esteemed too cheap for adver
tised heroism. Still some glowing tales could be told of
Wairarapa steerage bravery, notably one anent the saving
of 19 lives by a member of the foreoabin herd.
Dear Bulletin, — When working on Han-
nan’s, last year, I was camped near a W.A. man.
One night, when sitting at the fire, he asked me
where I came from. “Victoria,” I replied.
“Victoria,” he said, questioningly, “Is that part
of Australia?” After taking a minute or so to
recover my breath I told him that it was.
“H—1!” he said, “then you and I must be
countrymen.”—J.C.M., Coolgardie, 15th N0v.,’94.
The man who lately hung on to the handle
of the Duke of York’s carriage until the military
escort captured him, said that he only wanted te
shake hands with H.R.H. It wasn’t his luck to
be very drunk at the time, so the poor devil was
charged with lunacy. Everybody is supposed to
love the Royal Family, but any deadbeat who
gives color to this polite fiction is reckoned unsober
or mad.

CLEVELAND STREET SYDNEY
On the Beach.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Twelfth-night I remember, dear,
You were on these rugged stones.
Sweetest night of all the year,
And my heart repeats your tones,
Low and tender,
Such as render
Music when a mourner moans.
Setting sunbeams in your hair
Showed me first the bronzed thread
In the sable glory there
Compassing your seraph head,
Poised as Muse’s
When she loses
Self in song by passion led.
Downward glancing to your eyes,
Sweet, I saw a golden clime,
Flushed with lucent melodies
Of the spirit’s rosy prime;
Love unbidden,
Still half-hidden,
Wrote their music and their rhyme.
Still a little shall his songs
Lie unuttered in your eyes,
Till the hour to which belongs
Power to loose them shall aiise ;
Then their flowing,
Sweet as blowing
Of the horns of magic skies.
You shall never know the pain
In my heart that summer eve,
I would have it o’er again
Though to-morrow I should grieve ;
For the spirit
Doth inherit
Joys that melancholy leave.
Wellington, M.L.
HUBERT CHURCH.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His last bottle lay gleaming amongst the brittle,
yellow grass twenty miles back, and he had
drained it so well that after an hour the sugar-ants
no longer tumbled out of its narrow neck, clawing
inanely, and assaulting each other with a stupidity
almost human.
He walked quickly, fiercely, although the in-
tense, white sun seemed to concentrate its rays
upon his neck and between his shoulders, and
to follow him with the malignancy of a sentient
thing. His swag hung uncomfortably low, ana
the blackened billy dangled about his ankles.
The great yellow-winged grasshoppers twanged
against his blistering ears and his cracked and
bleeding lips, stinging him like blows from a cane;
and every now and again a twig snapped, and tore
a new wound in his way-worn feet. But he
heeded none of these things ; he was intent upon
outwalking the two quaint little _ strangers
dogging him like shadows. Only let him escape
them, and he might rest, and bathe his feet in the
cool waters of the creek—and sleep.
The country was now sparsely timbered, stony,
and a mere succession of small hills. The travel-
ing was heavy, and he had forgotten whither he
was bound and everything but the presence of his
tormentors. Curse them ! what did they want
with him ? But he would do them up. He was
the best man on the track from the Gulf to the
Murray ; surely he could outwalk them ! Then he
could camp, and, of course, he would remember
everything when his mind was at ease and he had
slept again.
They had amused him greatly when they came
upon him first near Starlight’s Rise —a. pair of little
men, exactly alike, and each with a little wooden
leg. He had laughed till his sides ached at their
tiny swags, their long beards almost sweep-
ing the ground, and, above all, at their
wooden legs ; but now he was wear3' of
them. Their white faces, and the awful jmalter-
able look of solemnity with which they regarded
him, filled his soul with a vague horror. He felt
that he would like to sneak upon them, and dash
their brains out against a tree ; but his attempts
to get nearer to them had proved quite futile.
They evaded him like ghosts, and now, no matter
how quickly he walked, when he looked over his
shoulder, there they were, stumping stolidly in
his tracks, apparently as fresh as when they
started in the morning.
Another peculiarity of the dumb strangers was
their idiotic manner of imitating his every action.
When he moved they moved; they turned, stopped
and gesticulated as he did, but always with
intense gravity. Their persistence was gradually
working him into a violent passion. He walked
quicker yet, dodging in and out amongst the
stunted timber. Presently he broke into a run,
and rushed headlong down a dry gully, looking
neither to the right nor to the left, spurred on by
his growing horror of the little men who haunted
him. Exhausted at length, he staggered against
an old, deformed gum, and rested upon its twisted
trunk. His eyes were seamed with red veins, and
flecks of mud-colored froth hung on his tangled
beard. ,
A ferocious malediction broke from the man s
lips : his mad race had been all in vain; there
were the two puny swagmen not 20 yards away,
imitating his attitude, and fixing him with their
great, grave eyes.
He threw off his swag, and ran at them, cursing
aloud, and showing his teeth like a dog. They
threw off their swags, and repeated his actions
and no effort on his part could lessen the distance
between them. He tore stones out of the ground,
and hurled them at his tormentors, furious with
rage. They threw stones back at him, and bur-
lesqued all his actions with solemn mockery, like
little devils in a pantomime. . ,
Worn out by his exertions and choking with
passion, he returned to his swag and sat down,
burying his face in his hands. The two little men
sat before him on their swags, each poking a wee
wooden leg out towards him, £ind both regarding
him patiently and mimicking every movement of
his limbs and every expression of his face. He
thought to draw them near him by cunning and
wiles; he spoke to them in tones of friendship,
and tried to smile through his parched lips. He
said he had a bottle of rum in his swag and invited
them to drink. They only stared at him with
stupid gravity and silently repeated his move-
ments. He paused on the brink of another out-
-1 burst of violent rage, holding the sling from his
swag in his hand. , ,
And now a brilliant scheme of vengeance flashed
I into his mind, and he laughed harshly. Hastily
detaching the long scarf, he turned and climbed
into the bent gum-tree, and with trembling, eager
hands tied one end of the sling to a convenient
limb. As he expected, the two little men imitated
his action and stood in another tree, facing him,
and waiting. He laughed again, fiercely, and tied
the other end of the sling in a noose about his
neck, watching to see that his enemies did like-
wise. The dwarfs stood ready. He uttered a
yell of triumph as he rolled from the limb, and as
he hung by the neck, swinging in mid-air, his
darkened eyes, bursting from their sockets, turned
to see the two little men kicking in the last agonies
of strangulation.

The Caligraph Writing Machine.
THE CALIGRAPH embodies all the highest
developments of applied mechanics, and its
principle having stood the test of 15 years, its
success towers above the fantastic devices of mush-
room competitors.
“I like Caligraph writing better than that of
any other typewriter because the type comes out
so sharply and clearly.” The prominent Anglican
clergyman, who used these words, had hit upon
the chief characteristic of the Caligraph without
exactly knowing what brought it about. The
result is attributable to the fact that platen of
the Caligraph being bevelled the type strike
against a flat surface thus producing a full and
even impression. When you see typewriting with
only the middle of the letters clear you know that
the machine used was not a Caligraph, but one
fitted with the ordinary round platen. When you
read a typewritten letter, with some of the type
leaning to the right and the rest to the left, you
may be sure that your correspondent has a crude
machine, with curved typebars, which naturally
become more crooked, and thus twist their type
out of perpendicular. Then remember that the
Caligraph has straight typebars which, like every-
thing else in the machine, are fitted with adjust-
able bearings.
The Caligraph manifolds 15 carbon copies at
one operation, and used with the Edison Mimeo-
graph reduplicates 1500 copies from one stencil.

“Forget-Me-N o t ."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I read in the book of my years
Three treasured words of the dear old days,
They are writ in a pure heart’s tears,
Yet they lead me back with their sunlit rays
To a time when I knew not care—
When Life and the World were fair.
Words of the Dead, tho’ mute they be,
I hear them at eve when the winds are low
And at rest in a reverie,
Like a voice from the grave of long ago,
Set to a strange, weird, dulcet key,
Singing a song of love to me.
Then to my heart I whisp’ring say :
“ Lo, thy sorrow has passed and all thy pain ”
So do I dream till far away
With thought in the past I am young again ;
Buoyant I tread the meads once more
J ust as I did in days of yore.
Christ of the Cross ! I only dream j
I am old and mourn, and my life is grey.
Lit by no bright, no friendly gleam
I wander alone in the night astray—
The dead are dead. Ah, stem old truth,
I have need for a word of ruth.
Shall I forget, withal, that face
As pure as the snow, and those perfect eyes
(Twin orbs that shone with winsome grace)
As bright and as blue as our summer skies,
Cancel the vow I onetime said:
Be Judas and forget my dead ?
I ask but if my heart speaks fair,
I know when we meet in the days supreme,
In the vast and fathomless Where,
We’ll meet as we did in the Past and dream,
Heart linked to heart and hand in hand,
I and my Dead in Love’s own land.
S.


A. D.. 1894.
Qy the return once more of the memorable season so universally observed beyond all
others as one of festivity and family re-unions, and in cases where distance intervenes celebrated
by friendly greetings and presentations, it is not a pleasant reflection to make that there is
always a section in every community unable so fully as their fellows to entei into the hilarity of
the time through physical disability. These demand our commiseration and help, ant., wc
may ask, what greater boon can be offered such people than
A Well-tried and Effective Remedy?
Bright’s Disease is wholly unlike any other disease, in that it produces geneia
derangement of the tissues of the body and involves other organs, even before the kidneys seem
to be disordered. It is recognised as a mysterious every-day malady which is increasing at
an alarming rate.
Dr. William Roberts, Professor in Owens College, Manchester, says: “The disease
proceeds silently amid apparent health.” If you suffer from disease of the heart, stomach or
nerves, at the back of all the trouble will be found diseased kidneys. This it is which in
reality depletes the energies, robbing the blood of albumen, poisoning the organism with uric
acid, and causing the heart, stomach and nervous complaints. The first symptoms of Bright’s
disease should be properly treated. Neglect entails either months of suffering and distress, or
suddenly ends in the grave. Measures for relief must be active, thorough and persistent,
is absolutely impossible to have pure blood and good health if the kidneys are not in proper
working order.
Warners “Safe” Cure
is the Only Remedy possessing the power to expel the uric acid and urea from the system,
as much as 500 grains being secreted every day.
Warners “Safe Cure
acts as an alterative in changing the abnormal tendency to degeneration of the epithelial and
glandular structures—enabling those tissues to excrete the full amount of waste products which

DO THOU LIKEWISE.
When one sets a candle in a window on a dark
night he never can tell how many lost and be-
wildered travellers it may guide on their way.
I once knew a dear lady who habitually did it
on every dark night. The road that passed her
house was always forsaken and lonesome enough,
and houses were far apart. So, on the general
principle of goodwill to men, she placed her
candle in a sort of projecting window, where it
would shine both ways and do the most good.
She is dead and gone now, but let us hope that
her family keeps up the custom. To be sure, it
brought her many a strange guest, yet she did
what she could for them, and never grumbled—
pay or no pay.
A year or two ago a little book was printed,
containing an account, by Mr. John Hodson, of
Warboys, Hunts, of the way he was cured of a
wasting disease by Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup—a complaint contracted in India, where
Mr. Hodson was once a soldier in the British
A rmy.
This candle of Hodson’s beamed hopefully in
all directions, and, among others, its rays fell
upon the eyes of a woman who needed light at
that particular time. How it came to pass she
tells m a letter, from which we quote the follow-
ing :
“ I was,” she says, “ always healthy up to
May, 1884, when I had an attack of gastric
'fever, which left me low and feeble. I had a
bad taste in the mouth, and after everything I
ate I had great pain in the chest and sides, and
also dreadful pam at the back and between the
shoulder-blades, and a sinking feeling at the pit
of the stomach. My legs trembled and shook
under me, so I could not walk out. Indeed, it
was as much as I could do to get across the
floor. A dry, hacking cough set in and shook
me very much, and I lost a deal of sleep.
“As I grew weaker I was confined to my bed
'more and more, and my daughter was obliged to.
take my place in doing the housework. A doctor
attended me for over a year. He tried first one
ikind of medicine and then another, but none of
them helped me. At the end of the year the
doctor said he could do no more for me, and
recommended me, as a last resort, to try a change
of air.
“I had got a despair of ever getting better
when a book was sent to me full of statements
from different people, telling how they had been
made well of various complaints by using Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and I read of a young
man living at Warboys, near me, having been
cured in a marvellous manner by this medicine.
I got a bottle from Messrs. Palmer and Sons,
chemists, Ramsey, and, after I had taken it a
week, I felt relief and gained strength. All my
pain left me, and I have never looked behind me
since. I take an occasional dose of the Syrup
and keep in excellent health. I give you full
permission to use this letter as you think fit.
Yours truly, (signed) Sarah Mason, Ramsey
Heights, Ramsey, Hunts, January 27th, 1892.”
What better use could we possibly put this
lady’s letter to than to publish it ? In this way
it has fallen into the present reader’s hands, and
may turn out to be a candle in the window to
shew him refuge and shelter beside some long and
gloomy path. A word more on this very point.
It is not a matter of choice whether we are to
help one another. We are obliged and hound to do
so by every consideration of morality and utility.
Humanity is like the body of one man : if a single
part is neglected, the rest must pay the penalty.
Therefore, set candles in windows. If, for
instance, Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup has
done you good, say so, publish it, make it known.
Others near you may be groping in darkness.
And it is all the more a subject of rejoicing that
this remedy, as in the above case, conquers indi-
gestion and dyspepsia, because that complaint it
is which clouds the sun for millions on Life’s
hard road.
A QUESTION.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
And so in the death-darkened chamber they
met—
The woman that once he had loved and the one
he loved yet—
The wife that had warped his desire and the
woman he could not forget !
They stood by the bier where between them he
slept,
And the love he had lost in his wife to her
swimming eyes leapt;
But the woman his life had belonged to—his
paramour—spoke not nor wept 1
It was only a story of sated desire—
Of a love merely sexual burnt to an ash by its
fire,
And a husband who turned to a more luscious
love that was his for the hire !
All had sinned. For the husband had killed by
his clutch,
Rough-handed, the fruit of a love that had
dropped at his touch !
His paramour’s sin was not loving, his wife’s
was in loving too much !
And so he had died ; it was over at last;
And across him the two women looked at each
other aghast—
Across his cold corpse, and across the cold
corpse of the loathsome dead Past !
Then the smouldering love of the wife leapt to
flame,
And she poured forth her kisses upon him, and
called on his name.
But the other said “ No, he is nothing to you,
soul and body I claim !”
They looked at each other awhile. Said the
wife wearily,
“He is mine ; for I loved him, and ever shall
love him ; let be !”
But the other sneered, “ No, he is mine, and
mine only, because he loved me !”
Then the two laid their hands on the body
between,
And fought for it, wife against paramour,
fiercely, unseen—
For the body diseased and polluted—as ever his
spirit had been !
And this is the question for answer in Hell:
To which of the women his body belonged, can
you tell ?
Think, was it the woman he loved, or the one
who had loved him too well ?
Maorilaud.

O’Hara, J.P .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
James Patrick O’Hara, the Justice of Peace,
He bossed the P. M. and he bossed the police;
A parent, a deacon, a landlord was he —
A townsman of weight was O’Hara, J.P.
He “gave out the prizes,” foundation-stones laid,
He shone when the Governor’s visit was paid ;
And twice re-elected as Mayor was he —
The flies couldn’t roost on O’Hara, J.P.
Now Sandy M'Fly, of the Axe-and-the-Saw,
Was charged with a breach of the licensing law —
He sold after hours whilst talking too free
On matters concerning O’Hara, J.P.
And each contradicted the next witness flat,
Concerning back parlors, side-doors and all that;
’Twas very conflicting as all must agree—
“ Ye’d betther take care ! ” said O’Hara, J.P.
But “ Baby,” the barmaid, her evidence gave —
A poor, timid darling who tried to be brave —
“ Now don’t be afraid—if it’s frightened ye be —
Speak out, my good girl,” said O’Hara, J.P.
Her hair was so golden, her eyes were so blue,
Her face was so fair and her words seemed so true —
So green in the ways of sweet women was he—
She jolted the heart of O’Hara, J.P.
He turned to the other grave Justice of Peace,
And whispered, “You can’t always trust the
police;
I’ll visit the premises during the day,
And see for meself,” said O’Hara, Jay Pay.
(Case postponed.)
’Twas early next morning, or late the same night
’Twas early next morning we think would be
right—
And sounds that betokened a breach of the law
Escaped through the cracks of the Axe-and-the-
Saw.
And Constable Dogherty, out in the street,
Met Constable Clancy a bit off his beat;
He took him with finger and thumb by the ear
And led him around to a lane in the rear.
He pointed a blind where strange shadows were
seen
Wild pantomime hinting of revels within —
“ We’ll drop on MTly if you’ll listen to me
And prove we are right to O’Hara, J.P.”
But Clancy was up to the lay of the land,
He cautiously shaded his mouth with his hand—
“ Wisht, man ! Howld yer whish ! —or it’s ruined
we’ll be,
It’s the justice himself —it’s O’Hara, J.P. !”
They hish’d and they whlshted and turned them-
selves round,
And got themselves off like two cats on wet
ground;
Agreeing to be, on their honor as men,
A deaf-dumb-and-blind institution just then.
Inside on a sofa, two barmaids between
And one on his knee, was a gentleman seen;
And any chance-eye at the keyhole could see
In less than a wink ’twas O’Hara, J.P.
The first in the chorus of songs that were sung,
The loudest that laughed at the jokes that were
sprung,
The guest of the evening, the soul of the spree—
The daddy of all was O’Hara, J.P.
And hard-cases chuckled, and hard-cases said
That Baby and Alice conveyed him to bed —
In subsequent storms it was painful to see
Those hard-cases side with the sinful J.P.
Next day, in the court, when the case came in
sight,
O’Ham declared he was satisfied quite ;
The case was dismissed —it was destined to be
The final ukase of O’Hara, J.P.
The law and religion came down on him first —
The Christian was hard, but his wife was the
worst!
Half ruined and half driven crazy was he —
It made an old man of O’Hara, J.P.
Now, young men who come from the bush, do you
hear ?
Who know not the power of barmaids and beer—
Don’t see for yourself! from temptation steer free,
Remember the fall of O’Hara, J.P.
HENRY LAWSON.

NEW SOUTH WALES GOVERNMENT RAILWAYS.
CHRISTMAS, 1894, & NEW YEAR, 1895.
A Holiday among the Mountains!
THE SEA COAST and COASTAL LAKES.
1
WEEPING
ROCK, WENTWORTH
BLUE MOUNTAINS.
FALuS,
The pleasures of an Australian holiday
have become largely enhanced by the
facilities afforded by the railways to the
dweller in the crowded, dusty city or
wide-spreading, arid plains of the interior
to escape to the cool, refreshing atmos-
phere of the mountain ranges, in the one
case, or the delicious breezes of the sea-
side in the other, where, forgetting the
toil and worry of business life, he obtains
the change of scene so necessary to the
recuperation of his physical and mental
powers. To those engaged in the routine
of country labour there can be no greater
enjoyment than that afforded by a holi
day trip to one of the beautiful seaside
resorts in which New South Wales is so
rich ; and the opportunity furnished by
the summer holiday season for enjoying
this pleasure causes the approach of
Christmas and the New Year to be hailed
with delight
by the sun-
browned
bushman,
who possesses
a wide choice
of picturesque
localities i n
which to pass
his holiday
hours.
To many
Sydney, with
its beautiful
surround-
ings, will
form an
irresistible
temptation,
for, apart
from its mul-
t i tudinou s
■*—
Msmm
ON THE HAWKESBURY.
attractions, it possesses much that is
interesting to the lovers of the pic-
turesque; its harbour abounding with
lovely spots, while within easy reach of
the heart of the city are broad sea
beaches and charming bays, washed by
the blue waters of the Pacific. North of
the metropolis is the Hawkesbury, per-
haps the most beautiful river in Austra-
lia, with its extensive reaches, unsurpassed
scenery, and ever-changing views of lofty
hill and luxuriant valley. Beyond this
stream is the extensive lake region of
which Brisbane Water, The Tuggerah
Lakes, and Lake Macquarie are the
principal features. Here the lovers of
boating and fishing may enjoy themselves
to their hearts’ content, while in the
surrounding country there is an endless
wealth of ferns and bush-flowers.
South of the metropolis, almost at its
very threshold, is the magnificent Na-
tional Park,
a charming
epitome of all
that is lovely
and impres-
sive in Aus-
tralian scen-
ery ; and a
few miles
beyond the
famous Illa-
war r a dis-
trict, the Gar-
den of New
South Wales,
the manifold
beauties of
which have
irresistibly
impressed
those who
have visited it. Here, too, are
the Bulli and Macquarie Passes:
Lake Illawarra; Clifton, Wollongong and
other sea-side resorts; Kiama, with its
great natural phenomenon, the “Blow
Hole;” Nowra, the metropolis of the
luxuriant Shoalhaven district; Kangaroo
Valley ; the Cambewarra and other lofty
mountain ranges, and other delightfully
picturesque localities. Every kind of
sea-side enjoyment —bathing, boating,
sailing, fishing, beach-rambles, etc. —may
be arranged at any of the coastal town-
ships or villages, where there is plenty of
good hotel accommodation at reasonable
rates. The Southern tourist district,
between Mittagong and Goulburn, is, in
many places, a land of enchantment.
Here are the celebrated Fitzroy and
Mery la Falls, the scenic glories of Mitta-
gong, Bandanoon, and other favorite halt-
ing places, with even grander landscapes
going beyond. From Goulburn enjoyable
trips may be made to the Wombeyan
Caves, or to Lake Bathurst and Lake
George, the latter being a notable sports-
men’s resort.
Then there are the Blue Mountains, a
realm of shady glens, umbrageous recesses,
rippling streams, silvery cascades, and all
the myriad charms with which Nature
enchants the lovers of the beautiful.
Katoomba and Wentworth Falls, the
stupendous Valley of the Grose, and
the thousand and one natural marvels,
have been brought by the railway within
the reach of the holiday-maker. Then
there are the Jenolan Caves, the great
subterranean wonder-world, which are
easily reached from Katoomba, Mount
Victoria, or Tarana. Everywhere the
holiday-maker, whether from town or
country, finds much that is lovely, re-
freshing and exhilarating in Nature placed
at his command, the tourist and excursion
arrangements by trains being made to
suit the means and convenience of every
class of holiday-seekers.
Holiday Excursion Tickets
Will be issued from all Stations to all Stations (suburban sections excepted),
commencing with the mail trains leaving terminal stations on 15th December,
1894, and thereafter by all trains until Ist January both days inclusive,
available lor return by ordinary trains until 31st January, 1895.
Special Excursion Tickets
Are issued on all Holidays at 2d. per mile First-class (minimum
ss) and Id per mile Second-class (minimum 2s. 6d.), from Sydney
and Suburban Stations to Goulburn, Newcastle,
Sinaleton Wollongong, Nowra, and intermediate stations ; also
io Sydney f rom Orange, Goulburn, Nowra, Newcastle,
nnrl intermediate stations.
